# [OT] Legislative Shenanigans

_291 messages · 19 participants · 2003-05_

---

### [OT] Legislative Shenanigans

- **From:** "JerryMouse" <nospam@bisusa.com>
- **Date:** 2003-05-13T09:18:22-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4T2dna3ZZYcwY12jXTWJkQ@giganews.com>`

```
As you may know, 50-odd Texas House Democrats skedaddled for parts unknown
yesterday bringing business in the legislature to a standstill for lack of a
quorum.

One Republican house member has already created a "most wanted" deck of
cards, a la Iraqi fugitives.

The Speaker of the House has ordered the Department of Public Safety
(troopers) and the Texas Rangers to arrest instanter any decamped members
and bring them to the capitol forthwith.

A large bunch of runaways is reported ensconced in a motel in Oklahoma,
taking their meals at a nearby Denny's. The motel is reportedly surrounded
by Texas Rangers (one*) in an unmarked car. A plane is standing by.
Extradition has been requested of the governor of Oklahoma and the feds have
been alerted. The Speaker of the House needs to fetch only nine of the
miscreants to reach a quorum.

Already a wanted poster/website is up:

http://www.homestead.com/derelictlegislators/

The main beef of the Democrats is a proposed redistricting plan that would
switch as many as ten U.S. congressional seats from Democrat control to
Republican. Plus the decision to quit serving grits in the House cafeteria.


-----
* In the early 20th Century a riot broke out in Galveston. The mayor sent an
urgent telegram to the headquarters of the Texas Rangers asking for
immediate assistance in quelling the disturbance. The next morning, a single
Ranger got off the train. The shocked mayor exclaimed: "There's only ONE of
you?!!"

The ranger replied: "Well, there's only one riot."
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: [OT] Legislative Shenanigans

- **From:** "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
- **Date:** 2003-05-14T14:34:12-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<vc53566lp323d4@corp.supernews.com>`
- **References:** `<4T2dna3ZZYcwY12jXTWJkQ@giganews.com> <3EC189B6.3010209@Knology.net> <b9te87$2ih$1@panix1.panix.com>`

```

<docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:b9te87$2ih$1@panix1.panix.com...
> In article <3EC189B6.3010209@Knology.net>,
> LX-i  <DanielJSNOSPAM@Knology.net> wrote:
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> system to change it or find another place more to your liking.'

There are some things one may have great difficulty
'learn[ing] to live with', as in 'a thing about which I have
thought the same for more than 25 years'.

Where the system is defective, 'work[ing] within' may be
nearly impossible. For example, the Constitution of the
United States describes a republican form of government
within a federal system. Article III delegates, to the
Judicial branch, the power to decide 'all cases ... arising
under this Constitution, the laws of the United States, and
....' Article VI, Clause 2, states that 'This Constitution, and
the laws of the United States which shall be made in
pursuance thereof; and ... shall be the supreme law of the
land ....' The use of 'pursuance' provides sufficient authority
for the judicial branch to review (judicial review) the laws
of the United States, to determine whether those laws shall
become the supreme law of the land, and this it has refused
to do. Furthermore, the Fifth Amendment, states that
'... [no person shall] be deprived of life, liberty, or property
without due process of law ....' It is in due process that
judicial review is mandatory, in that, no person should
be charged with the violation of, or cowered into obeying,
a law that is uncontitutional. Finally, the absence of
judicial review changes the form of government from
republican to authoritarian, thus requiring that the people
prove, to a reluctanct court, that a law is unconstitutional;
rather than, a willing court using its delegated power to
prevent the unconstitutional exercise of power by the
Congress.

Finding 'another place more to your liking' has its
difficulties, as well. The migration laws of Austrailia, for
example, provide, as I recall, eight categories. The
category that applies to this discussion is political
asylum. For Austrailia to accept the politically disaffected
of the United States would be to more than double its
population within a year. Thus, for Austrailia to publicly
grant political asylum to one, it would, thereby, risk the
consequences of granting asylum to many and, in time,
alter its own political and social structures and its
relations with the United States.

While I acknowledge that you did not say the alternatives
'learn to live with it, work within the system to change it or
find another place more to your liking' would be easy, it is,
nontheless, true that the alternatives offered may not
provide a solution to some problems.

> >
> >(I'd better stop before I really get started - this is CLC, not
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

```
"Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>

>  It is in due process that
> judicial review is mandatory, in that, no person should
> be charged with the violation of, or cowered into obeying,
> a law that is uncontitutional.

(I think you meant 'cowed' instead of 'cowered' -- but 'coward' also works.)

When a bill is passed, the resulting law is presumed constitutional and must
be followed until a court of competent jurisdiction nullifies the law in
question.

When someone says "I have been visited by the Holy Spirit and spook told me
this law is unconstitutional and therefore not to be obeyed," he is more
properly termed a Baptist than a legal scholar.
```

```

<docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:b9uu2u$7cm$1@panix1.panix.com...
> In article <vc53566lp323d4@corp.supernews.com>,
> Rick Smith <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote:
[snip]
> >There are some things one may have great difficulty
> >'learn[ing] to live with', as in 'a thing about which I have
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> thread which went on, some might say, far too long.

Ahh! The American colonist in 1776, having perceived
"a long train of abuses and usurpations" should have
'learn[ed] to live with it' by changing their thought?

It didn't work for them. It won't work for me.

[snip]
>
> >Article III delegates, to the
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> wrong again!'?

Sometimes I agree. Other times I disagree. In any case,
I learn.

This Fall I expect a determination as to whether one case
will be presented before a local inferior court. This will
involve a new interpretation of the Tenth Amendment
and a complaint that I was denied due process by the
absence of judicial review.

This Summer, with or without the aid of counsel, I plan on
filing suit against the United States, in Washington, D.C.,
on, possibly, two issues. One, the Ninth Amendment -- one,
seeking an injunction. I am pursuing other avenues that
may make the suits unnecessary.

Then, depending upon the outcomes, I will, once again,
agree or disagree. And, the system may be changed, or not.

> [snip]
>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> Americans, the expressing of a rather... curious taste.

It is a matter of perspective. American citizenship is
retained. Residence is granted until the reasons for
the political disaffection cease. As a guest, I need
only comply with the law of the host country. It does not
matter if the law is essentially the same. The host
country has not abused my rights and usurped my
powers. The central issue is physical separation from
the abusive environment.

That I perceive the environment as abusive need only
reflect my unique nature, an ability to see the pattern,
understand it and how it affects me, and to react to it.
My inability to accept or adapt to it may be unique,
as well, though I doubt it.

I finally decided to stay and fight, in the courts. It is the
American thing to do.
```

```
The Fall issue may be resolved before trial, for other
reasons, though I would prefer it to continue.

I do not expect public hearings, this Summer. Serving
notice is like setting off a firecracker, it gets their attention.
Before any hearing, the information will be examined by
their experts and either will be accepted, or ... well ...
accepted. Having accepted the information, action will be
taken. It is likely that the particulars will be seen only by
lawyers and experts; though I expect the resulting changes
to be significant and public.

> >
> >> [snip]
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> regarding a different... perspective about being subject to the Crown.

I do not perceive the situation to be that different. One
exception is that soldiers will not arrest me for libel
and sedition or other charges and therefore I will not
be subject to imprisonment or execution for exercising
freedom of speech.

I would characterize both governments as having
committed 'a long train of abuses and usurpations'
evincing a design to subject the people to the rule
of the government. The Crown was, at that time and
may be seen as, open about it because of its
presumed right; the U.S. Congress uses subterfuge
supported by the courts.

The result is similar, even if the means are different.
```

```
> [snip]
>
> >> Last time I looked, Mr Smith, *everything* is a 'matter of
perspective',
> >> including this statement.  Look back to those American colonists in
1776
> >> regarding a different... perspective about being subject to the Crown.
> >
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> torture and death for even *voicing* these opinions.

Having freedom of speech under an authoritarian
government is like talking about the weather, it doesn't
make any difference what one says, nobody can do
anything about it.

Under a government with strictly limited powers, nobody
will talk about the government, they will talk about the
weather, because the government will be less offensive
to the people than the weather.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: [OT] Legislative Shenanigans

_(reply depth: 11)_

- **From:** "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
- **Date:** 2003-05-16T12:46:47-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<vca5k6ora8o4c3@corp.supernews.com>`
- **References:** `<4T2dna3ZZYcwY12jXTWJkQ@giganews.com> <vc80cr3gvgal4b@corp.supernews.com> <ba1e25$a2k$1@panix1.panix.com> <vc8kodrccb28c3@corp.supernews.com> <ba2jrq$g5h$1@panix1.panix.com>`

```

<docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:ba2jrq$g5h$1@panix1.panix.com...
> In article <vc8kodrccb28c3@corp.supernews.com>,
> Rick Smith <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote:
> >
> ><docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message
news:ba1e25$a2k$1@panix1.panix.com...
> >> In article <vc80cr3gvgal4b@corp.supernews.com>,
> >> Rick Smith <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote:
> >> >
> >> ><docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message
news:ba067t$equ$1@panix1.panix.com...
> >>
> >> [snip]
> >>
> >> >> Last time I looked, Mr Smith, *everything* is a 'matter of
perspective',
> >> >> including this statement.  Look back to those American colonists in
1776
> >> >> regarding a different... perspective about being subject to the
Crown.
> >> >
> >> >I do not perceive the situation to be that different. One
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> irrelevancy.

The analogy was unclear. I should have said: Authoritarian
government is like the weather, everybody talks about;
but nobody can do anything about it.

Freedom of speech (and of the press) under an
authoritarian government is not without benefit. It allows
individuals to express their frustrations openly and
prevents a build-up of anger that could threaten the
emotional stability of those individuals. By itself,
freedom of speech (and of the press) has little or no
effect on government, only on people.

It is in the freedom to assemble and petition the
government for redress of greivances that government
(the executive and legislators) may see that their acts
have gone too far or that they have not done enough.

> >
> >Under a government with strictly limited powers, nobody
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> strictly limited powere', right?

Decisions regarding 'wet' versus 'dry' in regard to the
consumption of 'intoxicating liquors' occur in areas as
small as precincts. In these cases, the people, not
representatives, make the determination directly.
Enforcement is by the municipality in which the precinct
exists. Thus, at the precinct level, government power is
strictly limited by the non-existence of any representative
government.

Compare the above with the actions concerning the
Prohibition Amendment where Congress and the
States, 'to secure the blessings of liberty ...' decided
the importation, manufacture, and distribution of
'intoxicating liquors' shall be prohibited. With this
amendment and its attendent laws, the representatives
of the people, and not the people themselves, denied
the Ninth Amendment right, 'retained by the people',
to consume 'intoxicating liquors'.

Had the Supreme Court intervened under 'judical
review' to invalidate all laws enacted under that
amendment as 'denial of due process' the government,
for more than fourteen years, would not have hunted
down (with lethal force permitted), arrested, charged,
and convicted those who were satisfying the right of
the people to consume 'intoxicating liquors'.

The strict limitation on government power should have
been derived from  the Tenth Amendment ' reserved ...
to the people', in that it is the people, not their
representatives, who have the authority to delegate
power and that power is delegated only to the extent
that it is clearly and reasonably (i.e., expressly)
delegated; and that the representatives of the people
are impotent under the Ninth Amendment '... retained
by the people' to enact legislation denying or
disparaging rights. Thus, with respect to rights,
government can do no more than enact restrictions on
the free exercise of rights and only to the extent that
the unrestricted free exercise of a right of one
interferes with or violates the rights of another.

By this description of 'a government with strictly limited
powers', one may see that the laws of the United States
enacted with authority of the Prohibition Amendment
were unconstitutional and that some people, known
variously as gangsters and criminals, were 'deprived
of life, liberty, and property without due process of law'
and that some people, known as citizens, were denied
thier Constitutional right to consume 'intoxicating liquors'.
This was accomplished by special interest groups, known
as temperance unions, who used various means to
influence members of Congress and the several State
legislatures, excepting Connecticut and Rhode Island,
to enact and ratify an amendement and laws to cause
their views to be imposed upon all the people.

Thus there is, in precincts, 'a government with strictly
limited powers' and there can be, with appropriate
interpretation of the Constitution, 'a [representative]
government with strictly limited powers'. Because,
the acts of such governments have a direct relationship
to the delegated powers of those governments, the
people have a common understanding of compliance
and little to disagree about. If 'it goes without saying',
then it often goes unsaid. Where government is not stricly
limited, the people do not have a common understanding
or, perhaps, any understanding of the purpose for the acts
of government, and there is much to disagee about and
much discussion.

Therefore "[u]nder a government with strictly limited
powers, nobody will talk about the government, they
will talk about the weather, because the government
will be less offensive to the people than the weather."

You may notice that I had little to say about prohibition
in precincts and much to say about prohibition in the
larger scale. This is because I am not offended by the
people deciding for themselves -- I don't have to live
there. I am offended by representatives deciding for
the people -- I can't easily live elsewhere.
```

```
The 9th amendment doesn't make the 18th amendment "unconstitutional" any more
than it does the 14th.  (Our right to have slaves is not mentioned in the 9th).
The 9th amendment doesn't really say anything we can grasp our teeth into, it
just says that there are other rights.

A non-expressed, non-enforced right is meaningless anyway, as the only way we
can determine what is one's right is by what we can enforce.   I would start by
enforcing the Constitution, starting with the 10th amendment.
```

```
It may not say anything *you* can 'grasp your teeth into';
```

```
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> changes the constitution - be unconstitutional?

I never said the amendment was unconstitutional. I said
its attendent laws were unconstitutional.

Article V of the Constitution says, in part, that amendments
"shall be valid to all Intents and Purposes, as Part of this
Constitution".

Note that it does not say "the intent and purpose of the
amendment". Thus it is the text, and only the text, that
applies.

If the words do not alter the meaning of appropriate
parts of the Constitution, the original meaning is
unchanged.

Unfortunately, upon re-reading the Eighteenth Amendment
I saw, in the text, "... for beverage purposes ...."

I retract my statements concening the unconstitutionality
of the laws attendent to the Eighteen Amendment and
apologize for any confusion I may have caused.
```

```
On Fri, 16 May 2003 19:59:57 -0400, "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
enlightened us:

>
><docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:ba3qdu$nc2$1@panix1.panix.com...
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
>

And in that you are wrong.  They are only unconstitutional if a court
of law says they are with the Supreme Court of the United States
having the final say should they chose to do so.  If the law goes
unchallenged, then it cannot be assumed to be unconstitutional.  The
congress makes the laws.  The judicial branch upon challenge rules on
their constitutionality.


>Article V of the Constitution says, in part, that amendments
>"shall be valid to all Intents and Purposes, as Part of this
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>

You have taken that phrase out of context.  The whole phrase says, "
The Congress, whenever two thirds of both Houses shall deem it
necessary, shall propose Amendments to this Constitution, or, on the
Application of the Legislatures of two thirds of the several States,
shall call a Convention for proposing Amendments, which, in either
Case shall call a Convention for proposing Amendments, which, in
either Case, shall be valid to all Intents and Purposes as Part of
this Constitution, when ratified by the Legislatures of three fourths
of the several States, or by Conventions in three fourths thereof, as
the one or the other Mode of Ratification may be proposed by the
Congress;"

It describes the steps necessary to propose and adopt an amendment.
It does not describe what may or may not be in the text of that
amendment.

>If the words do not alter the meaning of appropriate
>parts of the Constitution, the original meaning is
>unchanged.
>

The original meaning may and will remain unchanged, but the effect of
the original is superseded by the later amendment.  Cases supporting
the later law are call precedents and are used to substantiate its
meaning when applied.  Just because an amendment remains on the
"books" does not mean it is still the law.


>Unfortunately, upon re-reading the Eighteenth Amendment
>I saw, in the text, "... for beverage purposes ...."
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Regards,

          ////
         (o o)
-oOO--(_)--OOo-

Next time you think you're perfect try walking on water.

Steve
```

```
And you have much to learn.

Is an innocent person (not guilty of the offense charged
or lesser offenses) guilty because the court says they
are guilty, or did the court decide that the force of
law will be used to punish the person?

Any individual may find that a law is unconstitutional
by asserting a particular meaning to the Constitution.
It is the Supreme Court that will decide whether that
meaning, or another, will prevail and thus whether the
law has force.

Courts cannot make the innocent guilty; but can cause
the person to be punished as if they were. The Supreme
Court cannot make an unconstitutional law constitutional;
but can decide that it be enforced as if it were.

Constitutional (of or relating to the Constitution) is a
matter of individual opinion. Whether a law is to have
force is a matter for the courts.

>
>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> You have taken that phrase out of context.  The whole phrase says, "
[snip]

of the text and supplied the necessary and proper context
for my comments.

…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> "books" does not mean it is still the law.

What? If the original meaning is unchanged, its effect
cannot have been superseded!
```

```
Mr Smith, while the phrase 'innocent until proven guilty in a court of 
law' is a cornerstone of Anglo-Saxon jurisprudence you might want to do a 
bit of learning yourself.  A court does not find innocence, it assesses 
guilt; people are found to be guilty or not guilty.

Note the phrase 'found to be'... the usual formulation is a variant of 
that, 'You have been found guilty of...'

Not that you *are* guilty of, or not guilty of - no innocence involved at 
all - but 'found' guilty.
```

- **References:** `<4T2dna3ZZYcwY12jXTWJkQ@giganews.com> <vcd7ms3b94u5ff@corp.supernews.com> <ba6q6l$69b$1@panix1.panix.com> <vce3eorb49f4ef@corp.supernews.com>`

```
In article <vce3eorb49f4ef@corp.supernews.com>,
Rick Smith <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote:
>
><docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:ba6q6l$69b$1@panix1.panix.com...
…[36 more quoted lines elided]…
>the guilt, or not, of a defendent in court.

I barely am aware of what *I* am aware of, Mr Smith, let alone what anyone 
else is aware of... as for what 'people' seem to prefer, I leave that to 
them.

Given that your original sentence 'Is an innocent person (not 
guilty of the offense charged or lesser offenses) guilty because the court 
says they are guilty...' places a burden of responsibility on court 
proceedings ('... because the court says') then I do not understand how 
you can avoid this predication ('the question is... not the guilt, or not, 
of a defendant in court') when an error is pointed out; this seems to 
violate the Principle of Non-Contradiction.  Consider:

'This situation is because of what happens in (x).'

'(x) does not perform that function.'

'The question is not what happens in (x).'

'How can something be 'because of (x)' and not be concerned with 'what 
happens in (x)'?  This appears to be saying that something *can* both be 
and not-be the same thing in regards to the same aspect at the same time; 
this seems to violate the Principle of Non-Contradiction.'

DD
```

```
courts or their proceedings.

different than they are, the Court cannot make an
unconstitutional law different than it is. But both courts
and the Court can adjudicate these things as if they
were different than they are.
```

```
><docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:ba84ko$q7m$1@panix1.panix.com...
…[71 more quoted lines elided]…
>courts or their proceedings.

Mr Smith, the Constitution is a legal document.  (side note - the 
Declaration of Independence is not)  The province of determining the scope 
and applicability of a legal document is that of the courts; otherwise 
what you propose is a sort of idiosyncratic anarchy.

'I say it is constitutional!'

'I say it isn't... so there!'

>
>By understanding that courts cannot make things be
>different than they are, the Court cannot make an
>unconstitutional law different than it is.

Mr Smith, constitutionality is determined by the courts, remember?  It is 
their decisions which allow the label of 'constitutional' to be applied.

>But both courts
>and the Court can adjudicate these things as if they
>were different than they are.

Mr Smith, legal matters exist *only* in the adjudication thereof; to speak 
otherwise begins to wander into Platonism.

DD
```

```
> >> Mr Smith, legal matters exist *only* in the adjudication thereof; to

> >> otherwise begins to wander into Platonism.
> >
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> the status of 'constitutionality'.

Mr Dwarf, the dispute seems to center on the use of
'constitutional' in two senses: constitutional as a matter
of law and constitutional as a matter of opinion. I was
careful to distinguish between these two, though I did
not do so explicitly.

I believe you have, in your objections, relied upon
'constitutional as a matter of law', exclusively; and, in
your doing so, deny that 'constitutional as a matter of
opinion' has any validity or relevance to this discussion
or elsewhere; and therefore, it is not unreasonable for
me to infer from your objections that I, and other
individuals, are not, and should not be, permitted to
form such opinions about government.

Repeated from above:
> >> >By understanding that courts cannot make things be
> >> >different than they are, the Court cannot make an
> >> >unconstitutional law different than it is.
> >>
> >> Mr Smith, constitutionality is determined by the courts, remember?  It
is
> >> their decisions which allow the label of 'constitutional' to be
applied.

This is a clear example of your restricting the use of
'constitutional' to that which is determined by the courts
and by implication a matter of law. This does not simply
disagree with my use of '[un]constitutional', it rejects
every notion that I have any right to express an opinion
about the 'constitutionality' of any law, regulation, process
of law, etc., because such opinion will not have been
'determined by the courts'.
```

```
held on some other charge while the detectives dig up more evidence,
```

```
found
> > >> guilty (both statuses pending appeal).
> > >
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> double jeopardy?

This is not likely to answer your question; but you might
find it ineteresting, nonetheless.

< http://www.access.gpo.gov/congress/senate/constitution/toc.html >
Download Document con016.pdf - 125 pages

This document describes the interpretation of the Fifth
Amendment as developed by the Supreme Court.

The history, theories, and explanations of "Double Jeopardy"
begin on PDF page 9 and continue through PDF page 32.

This quote is from PDF page 13:

"During the 1970s especially, the Court decided an uncommonly
large number of cases raising double jeopardy claims.[62] Instead of
the clarity that often emerges from intense consideration of a particular
issue, however, double jeopardy doctrine has descended into
a state of 'confusion,' with the Court acknowledging that its decisions
'can hardly be characterized as models of consistency and clarity.'"
```

```
public.
```

```
On Sat, 17 May 2003 16:37:45 -0400, "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
enlightened us:

>
>SkippyPB <swiegand@neo.NOSPAM.rr.com> wrote in message
…[45 more quoted lines elided]…
>

The court decides if the evidence presented before it proves whether
the charged person is guilty or not guilty of a crime.  The burdon of
proof is upon the state, thus making true the statement, "innocent
until proven guilty".  Punishment is a separate phase of the system
and only comes into play when the person is found guilty.  The law is
and of itself is not a "force".  It is a set of rules.  If you disobey
those rules, the possible punishments are spelled out in the law.  The
courts must follow what the legislature allows.  They cannot take it
upon themselves to give less than a minimum or more than a maximum
unless.  Even in the case of a suspended sentence, this is followed.


>Any individual may find that a law is unconstitutional
>by asserting a particular meaning to the Constitution.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>

An individual may "think" that a law is unconstitutional, but that
matters little.  Only a court can decide if a law is uncostitutional
or nor.  

>Courts cannot make the innocent guilty; but can cause
>the person to be punished as if they were. The Supreme
>Court cannot make an unconstitutional law constitutional;
>but can decide that it be enforced as if it were.
>

Courts can and do make the innocent guilty.  Happens all the time.
Why you think otherwise is beyond me.  If the state proves its case
(right or wrong) and the defense can't counter, then a person can be
found guilty.  As I said before, punishment is the next phase.  You
cannot lump them together.

The Supreme Court could  make an unconstitutional law constitutional.
They could overturn a ruling made by an earlier Supreme Court. 
 
>Constitutional (of or relating to the Constitution) is a
>matter of individual opinion. Whether a law is to have
>force is a matter for the courts.
>

It is NOT a matter of individual opinion.  It is a state of a law that
only the Supreme Court can make.

>>
>>
…[31 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Yes it can and is all the time.

Regards,

          ////
         (o o)
-oOO--(_)--OOo-
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: [OT] Legislative Shenanigans

_(reply depth: 16)_

- **From:** "JerryMouse" <nospam@bisusa.com>
- **Date:** 2003-05-17T13:53:39-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<q5WdnRnSgMGsGFujXTWJhw@giganews.com>`
- **References:** `<4T2dna3ZZYcwY12jXTWJkQ@giganews.com> <vca5k6ora8o4c3@corp.supernews.com> <eImcnd2x64Xj_VijXTWJjQ@giganews.com> <vcapu0e9hij9b9@corp.supernews.com> <ba3qdu$nc2$1@panix1.panix.com> <vcav0gtkmg8e2b@corp.supernews.com>`

```

"Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
> >
> > >> A constitutional amendment and laws necessary to enforce it cannot be
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> > Here, Mr Smith, is what I see as the core of the argument.  How can it
be,
> > as you assert, that a constitutional amendment - which, by definition,
> > changes the constitution - be unconstitutional?
>
> I never said the amendment was unconstitutional. I said
> its attendent laws were unconstitutional.

Except, of course, for those amendments which read: "The Congress shall have
the power to enforce this article by appropriate legislation?"

When a bill becomes law, the law is presumed constitutional and retains that
status until ruled otherwise by a court of competent jurisdiction. No law,
per se, is unconstitutional. If it's a law, it's constitutional; if it's not
constitutional, it's not a law. There is no such thing as an
unconstitutional law.

>
> If the words do not alter the meaning of appropriate
> parts of the Constitution, the original meaning is
> unchanged.
>

Not exactly. It is not the 'words' of an amendment that alter the original
meaning - it is the sole responsibility of the courts to interpret (alter)
the meaning of the words.

For example, if the Supreme Court held the words "... shall not be
infringed." in the 2nd Amendment simply meant we could not  attach lace,
strings, banners, or other 'fringe' to our guns, we'd be screwed without a
single recourse - save for removing the judges via impeachment and trying
again.
```

```
There is far too much concerning 'interpretation', in
"The Federalist", to quote here. So I only mention it
as a reference. But see, in particular, the quote from
a letter by Robert Yates.

It has long been accepted by the Court that it is the
right of the people to alter or abolish the Constitution.
How are the people to understand whether the
Constitution should be altered, if they do not first
find their own interpretation of the Constitution and
apply that to the law?

As I see it, it is a responsibility of citizens to interpret
the Constitution to determine if the system is working.
I have found that it is not. I have also found that it is
necessary to restore that which the Court has decided
is ineffective.

It we rely, solely, upon the Court to tell us what the
Constitution means then we submit ourselves to its
authority as the 'elite' in an authoritarian government.
```

```
Less a definition than an aphorism or, perhaps, an

> >

…[31 more quoted lines elided]…
> what you assert here.

I intended no particular order or emphasis. I was merely
expressing my opinion.

> >

…[5 more quoted lines elided]…

back
> >> it, though, because there's never been such a thing as 'a government
with
> >> strictly limited powere', right?
> >
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> investigate from there.

In the above, I used 'powers' and 'offensive' to restrict
my observation to the ways in which government
operates or accomplishes its ends.

It has been my observation that, locally, the talk is of
roads, schools, etc., that is, the services of government
and far less the government, itself. Local governments,
generally, have strictly limited powers.

The local homeowner's association, which had no real
power, was seldom discussed, though the community
was. Recently, the homeowner's association proposed
an amendment to the covenants and restrictions that
would have provided the homeowner's association
with a general welfare power; that is, to spend and
effectively, tax the homeowners. Within a few months,
during which debate was vocal and heated, the
amendment was trashed and the homeowner's
association disbanded.

In a community newspaper, that I read occassionally,
the letters to the editor (I find them comedic) are mostly
about the politics in Washington, D.C., less about the
state or county. Recently, one writer demanded that
Congress stop violating the Constitution and protect
Social Security.

My comment was based upon my observations.
Perhaps, these observations are biased and my
comment should be treated as my opinion, as well.
```

```
Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message

I don't quite understand your question. Did you mean
"can give (or allow) freedom of speech"?

If that is what you meant, then the answer is the Untied
States.

While the United States is, constitutionally a republic,
there were a series of acts of Congress and decisions
by the Supreme Court that amounted to a reconstruction
of the Constitution to greatly expand the power of the
United States to control the people.

Authoritarian is defined (http://www.m-w.com) as
"of, relating to, or favoring a concentration of power in
a leader or an elite not constitutionally responsible to
the people". What I have been saying, in effect, is that
Congress and the Court are the elite and that the
reconstruction no longer makes this elite responsible
to the people.

I talk more about freedom of speech in another
response in this thread.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ [OT] Now for a totally different perspective (was: Legislative Shenanigans

_(reply depth: 13)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2003-05-16T13:15:12-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ba39rm$plg$1@slb0.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<4T2dna3ZZYcwY12jXTWJkQ@giganews.com> <vc74do3lti1m19@corp.supernews.com> <ba067t$equ$1@panix1.panix.com> <vc80cr3gvgal4b@corp.supernews.com> <ba1e25$a2k$1@panix1.panix.com> <vc8kodrccb28c3@corp.supernews.com> <ba2sjr$dij$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <vca7cjib8j7j11@corp.supernews.com> <ba37fv$hom$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`

```
Given the predominance of posts by those (whose freedom to express their
opinions, I fully support) who seem to oppose governmental "welfare",  I
thought that I would express my own PERSONAL opinion.

Emotionally (philosophically?)  I consider my personal category to be a
"theoretical Christian Communist".  I personally LIKE the goal (no matter
how impractical) of:

 ""from each according to his/her ability, to each according to his/her
need"

I believe that the MOST important responsibility of any government is to use
*my* contribution (tax, resources, whatever) to meet the needs of those who
are unable (for WHATEVER reason) to achieve a "reasonable" (how subjective
can you get?) existence.  I think that education of the young (and those
previously deprived of adequate education) is as critical a governmental
responsibility as insuring the health, adequate shelter, and food of all of
its citizens.

I won't go into specific details on my reasoning (or more accurately
emotions) that has lead me to this stance.  Neither will I "justify" my
opinions.  I certainly won't be surprised by the majority of the posters to
this NG disagreeing with me (and I probably won't reply to must such posts).
HOWEVER, I did think that I should at least post my views - to give the NG
an understanding that there are those (or at least one) who participate in
comp.lang.cobol who believe in "government welfare" as well as governmental
responsibility for "social justice".
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: [OT] Now for a totally different perspective (was: Legislative Shenanigans

_(reply depth: 15)_

- **From:** "Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2003-05-16T20:17:51+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ba3h1f$lfg$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`
- **References:** `<4T2dna3ZZYcwY12jXTWJkQ@giganews.com> <vc74do3lti1m19@corp.supernews.com> <ba067t$equ$1@panix1.panix.com> <vc80cr3gvgal4b@corp.supernews.com> <ba1e25$a2k$1@panix1.panix.com> <vc8kodrccb28c3@corp.supernews.com> <ba2sjr$dij$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <vca7cjib8j7j11@corp.supernews.com> <ba37fv$hom$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <ba39rm$plg$1@slb0.atl.mindspring.net> <3EC53A93.2D02592C@shaw.ca>`

```
Really a state should look at both goals and what actually works.   Generally
they make compromises between the two.

If the state had a 100% tax to pay for universal care - the amount of money
available for universal care would be lower than if they used a 20% tax, as
people stopped having taxable income.    And if we allowed nothing that could
harm the environment, we would quickly revert to starvation and move towards a
slash and burn economy.    These are obvious extremes.

What we end up though is something between a policy that maximizes long term tax
revenues via economic growth, and one with slow economic growth that maximizes
short term tax revenues.   Most people believe this is a good thing.

But we rarely consider costs correctly.   What's a billion dollars?   A billion
dollars spent on disease research is a billion dollars not spent on schools.   A
billion dollars spent on bombs is a billion dollars not spent on machine tools.
 A billion dollars spent on prisons is a billion dollars not spent on preparing
the poor for careers.

What's wrong with welfare?   To some conservatives, it is "They need to have my
values and work".    I reject this argument, but I do recognize that getting the
poor working is good for all - if they can.   If a 95 year old man is starving,
I think we should feed him, even though we will receive no return on the
investment.   But it will mean that I have less money to spend on, say fire
protection.
```

```
"William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com> wrote in message news:ba39rm$plg$1@slb0.atl.mindspring.net...

>

Well, if you take that statement literally, I come pretty close to agreeing with you.
When you add one little caveat - that is each PERSON should be able to decide
what his ability is and how much of it he/she wants to exercise.

The 'to' part then balances the 'from' part, sooner or later.

It's a pretty good system, and has worked everywhere I can think of that it has
been given a fair chance; it's called by various names too- capitalism, free economy, entrepreneurial spirit, etc.

I expect that isn't quite what you had in mind, but like I said, taking it literally! :)

The real problem with Communism is not that it is basically anti-survival behavior for individuals (which, by the way, it *is*) but
that each and every time communism has taken hold and grown somewhere, the people have invariably become just playtoys for some
power mad, usually crazy, would be dictator. I surely think the world would have been better off WITHOUT such nut cases as Stalin,
and his so far to the right it is almost unbelievable contemporary, Hitler. I could do without most of the politicians I have met
too.
```

- **References:** `<4T2dna3ZZYcwY12jXTWJkQ@giganews.com> <vc74do3lti1m19@corp.supernews.com> <ba067t$equ$1@panix1.panix.com> <vc80cr3gvgal4b@corp.supernews.com> <ba1e25$a2k$1@panix1.panix.com> <vc8kodrccb28c3@corp.supernews.com> <ba2sjr$dij$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <vca7cjib8j7j11@corp.supernews.com> <ba37fv$hom$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <ba39rm$plg$1@slb0.atl.mindspring.net>`

```
A good post, Bill.

I, for one, respect your view.

Comments below...

"William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com> wrote in message
news:ba39rm$plg$1@slb0.atl.mindspring.net...
> Given the predominance of posts by those (whose freedom to express their
> opinions, I fully support) who seem to oppose governmental "welfare",  I
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>
This famous credo from Karl Marx has an inner beauty that has inspired many,
and precipitated momentous events in the 20th century.

It is so powerful that (like Kennedy being shot) I remember exactly where I
was when I first heard it. I was sitting on the grass in Albert Park in
Auckland, on a summer afternoon listening to a member of the Communist Party
as I figured I should at least understand what they believed before
dismissing them... I was 18 years old.

When I heard this, I was stunned. It is so simple and yet so powerful.

Sadly, it is also impossible. (You know as well as I do that as long as
Human Beings exist, some of us will succumb to the baser instincts that
prevent such an ideal from being implemented... greed, envy, arrogance,
selfishness, all that stuff that we constantly try and deal with, with
varying degrees of success.)

Doc raised the question of "who decides "need" "...

I heard it explained in a more humourous way...

Commissar: "So you have heard and understood Comrade Marx's basis for the
Communist doctrine?"

Peasant: "Yes, Comrade Commissar..."

Commissar: "So, if you have two houses and I do not have one, you would give
me one of yours?"

Peasant: "Most certainly, Comrade Commissar..."

Commissar: "And if you have two cars, and I do not have one, you would give
me your second car...?"

Peasant: "Definitely, Comrade Commissar. I would do so gladly, just as
Comrade Marx has prescribed..."

Commissar: "And if you have two cows, you would give me one, also...?"

The peasant drops his head and shifts from foot to foot before mumbling:
"No, Comrade Commissar..."

Commissar: "But why not, Man, you already agreed to give me your house and
your car?"

Peasant: "Yes Comrade Commissar, but I actually HAVE two cows..."

> I believe that the MOST important responsibility of any government is to
use
> *my* contribution (tax, resources, whatever) to meet the needs of those
who
> are unable (for WHATEVER reason) to achieve a "reasonable" (how subjective
> can you get?) existence.  I think that education of the young (and those
> previously deprived of adequate education) is as critical a governmental
> responsibility as insuring the health, adequate shelter, and food of all
of
> its citizens.
>

We all have our priorities and most of them are worthwhile. Unfortunately,
the pot is not infinite. Something has to suffer.
If we agree to live together collectively, we have to agree to help each
other out. This becomes easier if the GNP is large and we can see that our
nation is achieving something. There IS an argument that says if the wealth
of the country is huge, there should be no need for any of its citizens to
be deprived of at least the basic necessities of life. It gets much harder
when the resources are mismanaged, the national debt goes through the roof ,
and the bureaucrats attempt to balance the budget by raising taxes. The
underlying arguments are far too complex to cover here, but Marx's credo is
no more effective than Keynesian economics in dealing with them...


> I won't go into specific details on my reasoning (or more accurately
> emotions) that has lead me to this stance.  Neither will I "justify" my
> opinions.  I certainly won't be surprised by the majority of the posters
to
> this NG disagreeing with me (and I probably won't reply to must such
posts).
> HOWEVER, I did think that I should at least post my views - to give the NG
> an understanding that there are those (or at least one) who participate in
> comp.lang.cobol who believe in "government welfare" as well as
governmental
> responsibility for "social justice".
>
Glad you did, Bill.

I support your position but I get overruled by pragmatism <G>

Still, there is nothing wrong with idealism and sometimes it does make the
World a better place.

Pete.
```

```
That change was kind of bypassed.  Constitutionally, slaves still count 3/5 on
the census for determining how many representatives a state has.   But
Constitutionally, there are no more slaves.

I am a strong constitutionalist.  I believe the government works best when the
rulers believe that they are bound by limits.   When they believe in a
Constitution (which can be changed via hard work and time), we are safer from
their excesses.

Of course, around the world there are many states that ignore their
Constitutions altogether - and the US is getting closer and closer to this all
the time.
```

```
Doc, right now tax money is taken effectively by force from Canadian
taxpayers including myself and my wife to in part fund her salary as a
teacher.  Other members of the family are also employed by various
levels of government.  I have enjoyed riding both Via and Amtrak at the

political process into which we have limited input is to note the
obvious.  It is not being pejorative to note that in many ways someone
responsible to over 1000 individuals (IIRC a Canadian member of
parliament is responsible to over 40,000 and a US member of the House of
Representatives is responsible to over 500,000) on thousands of
decisions each year is responsible to nobody in many ways.  If any
elected representative satisfies the majority of his/her constituents
over 30 per cent of the time, I would be amazed.  Of course most of us
don't know what our elected representatives are doing on the majority of
legislative acts they deal with.  Further it would be almost impossible
for any one representative to be more than faintly knowledgeable about
most of the things they are called upon to deal with.  The system is
imperfect and coercive in many ways.  It is still better than any
alternative I have seen.
```

```
James J. Gavan wrote:
> So shoot the seniors and abort the mothers. As a pair that should keep the
> ammunition makers and pharmaceutical manufacturers real happy !

I don't advocate that - I just think that it's a shame that our 
government has propagated this scam called Social Security on the 
American people.  If a mutual fund had the return on investment that 
this program has (which isn't really invested all that much - current 
payouts come from current collections.  This was great for the "greatest 
generation" when they were dividing collections from the "baby boomers" 
amongst themselves.  Now, the "baby boomers" have figured out that 
they've been had - they weren't investing for their retirement, they 
were bankrolling other's retirement.  Now, they've aborted so many Gen 
X'ers that there aren't as many working adults paying into the system, 
and their benefits go nowhere near as far as the ones they funded.

Now, take the amount of money they paid into the system and let them 
invest that in solid companies.  (International Business Machines and 
DuPont are two that come to mind that have been around a long time and 
have continually grown - my biggest investment regret was selling the 
DuPont I inherited when I did - I could have over $100K from that now, 
which isn't bad for someone as young as I am.)  Now, these people are 
funding their -own- retirement, and they're not burdening the younger 
workers with the bill.

And on that note, I'm surprised the race warlords in this country 
haven't picked up on the racial aspects of this.  Black males have, on 
average, a significantly shorter life span that white females.  So, in 
effect, we have "the man" taking money from hard-working black males and 
giving it to white females.  Where's the outrage?

> On the other hand the Feds could take a little ditsy bit off the Defense
> budget. Still they might not then want 'boys in blue' writin' COBOL ?

Providing for the national ("common" in the Constitution) defense is one 
of the Constitutionally-mandated functions of our Federal government.  I 
think anyone who's paid attention to what's gone on in Afghanistan and 
Iraq over the last year and a half would agree that our defense spending 
is right on, and maybe ought to be a little higher (as we're now 
starting to get a bit of a pinch with missiles and such).  We defend our 
nation (and, in the process, other nations) through our unparalleled 
military power (and as part of coalitions with other nations).  (That 
way the other people can be free to worry about their Social Security... 
  ;) )

In my opinion, national defense and internal infrastructure are two of 
the best uses of my tax money.  Social programs are, for the most part, 
not a good use of my tax money.  Let these programs exist through 
charity, and let these artists earn their keep in the free market just 
like the rest of us.  :)
```

```
deeply, if a politician started trying to turn things around, they're 
decried as "hating" kids/seniors/minorities/etc.  Remember the plank of 
the Republican platform that called for abolishing the Department of 
Education?  It wasn't that they didn't care about kids, they just 
believed two things...

1)  The states know better what parts of their education systems need 
more attention
2)  The states would actually get more money if the bureaucratic office 
in Washington wasn't eating up funds

But for some reason, they never could get that message out over the 
Democrats' screaming foul...  It's a shame.
```

- **References:** `<4T2dna3ZZYcwY12jXTWJkQ@giganews.com> <3EC189B6.3010209@Knology.net> <b9te87$2ih$1@panix1.panix.com> <3EC28EDE.C8467E13@istar.ca> <FAEwa.297$vk3.68131@twister.austin.rr.com> <joe_zitzelberger-86822F.08405315052003@corp.supernews.com> <3EC42356.9040708@Knology.net> <3EC42ECF.DF9CA73A@shaw.ca> <3EC4402B.2050708@Knology.net> <joe_zitzelberger-B97CC9.00224116052003@corp.supernews.com> <3EC4D049.9090401@Knology.net>`

```
I worked for the US Department of Education .. by coincidence right there in
Montgomery (in the Saranoff Building). Before I started I would have agreed with
you. No Constitutional permission to regulate Education, therefore should be the
provenance of the States. 

My system dealt with student loans, which did not control education, it was
strictly money lending .. little different from what the Federal Reserve does
with its bonds. 

Fifty percent of 15M/yr US student loans go through traditional private sector
sources; the other fifty percent go through the DOE Direct Loan program I worked
on.

We charged a fee for originating and processing each loan. It approximated our
actual expenses and returned a small surplus to the US Treasury. Private lenders
did the same. Our fee was about half the private sector's. They kept complaining
they couldn't compete because our fee must somehow be subsidized. It wasn't. We
were simply more efficient and more professional. 

For instance, our loan origination was completely automated via a Web site,
including the credit check and all-important signature on the loan document. We
got a valid electronic signature. The private sector offered nothing resembling
that. They were still shuffling and filing paper the old fashioned way. 

The DOE people who visited every few months from their headquarters in the DC
area (Ballston, Md) were some of the most intelligent and professional people
I've had the pleasure of working with. They weren't anything like my high-school
teachers. They weren't like teachers at all. They knew the system inside out and
they knew the methodology used to create it. One of them was sexy as all get
out.

The experience changed my opinion about Federal involvement. We were
head-to-head against the private sector, on a level playing field, and we
prevailed because we were better. 

My experience with local education administration has not been salubrious. They
ARE like my dim-witted high-school teachers. 

--- history below ---
```

```
Well spoken, Daniel, and I understand your concerns.

Here's a thought...

"(as we're now starting to get a bit of a pinch with missiles and such).  "

I understand tomahawks cost over $1,000,000 each. In Afghanistan they were
used to target the homes of the Taliban and Al Queida leadership.

Now, I don't know what real estate costs in Afghanistan, but wouldn't it
have been much cheaper to just buy the properties and send in the
bulldozers...<G>?

After Iraq I guess the inventory is showing serious depletion. Of course, if
nuclear weapons had been deployed that would not have been a problem. (It
has been calculated that full scale Global nuclear War could not last more
than 4 days due to the rapid destruction of infrastructure and resources;
after 4 days there's nothing left to fight with or over...- The Third World
War -). As nuclear weapons were NOT deployed, the game came down to
conventional weapons. As noted, these weapons are becoming horrifically
expensive.

So, next time the US decides to (rightly or wrongly) get involved in a War,
why not get the potential combatants round a table, drink a few beers (or
coffee, if they're non-alcoholic types), play a few hands of poker, and
agree that the conflict will be limited to bows and arrows?

This would give the under-priveleged nations as much right to fight a war as
the technically advanced ones, and everyone would give great kudos to the US
for being so sporting... Of course it would set Humanity back around 500
years, but as we still seem Hell bent on War as a solution to our
differences, that is no more than we deserve <G>.

Another thought I had that could save a lot of resource, was that the Armed
Forces should be composed of people over 50. (The ones who have made their
contribution and are therefore expendable). We should be PROTECTING the
potential reformers, movers and shakers of Society, (the youth), not sending
them off to War...

Sergeant: "You asleep on duty again, Smith?"

Smith: "It ain't my fault, Sarge. You scheduled the patrol during my nap
time... Anyway, none of the boys are up to hiking through minefields. Wilson
lost his walking stick, Johnson can't move with arthritis,  Brown tried to
pull the pin out of a grenade with his teeth, like he saw Audie Murphy do in
the movies...ended up throwing his teeth at the enemy and the exploding
grenade took out him and Simpson... we've had a hard day."

Sergeant: "Yeah, I guess you're right. Let's pack it in and head back to
base...now where the Hell is that base...Christ, this Alzheimers is a
curse..."


> In my opinion, national defense and internal infrastructure are two of
> the best uses of my tax money.  Social programs are, for the most part,
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I believe you may modify this position as you get older, Daniel. (I did
<G>).

Sadly, there are always those in our Society who simply cannot "earn their
keep in a free market".

It would be immoral (given that we live in an affluent society) to simply
let these people die. If a nation generates so much wealth, there is an
argument that says it should care for at least the basic requirements of all
of its citizens.(that includes the freeloaders, deadbeats, no-hopers, and
anti-socials, simply by right of birth. Hey, the law protects the right of
children to their birthright, why not Society protecting its "children"?)

Suppose that all Social programs were based on charity as you suggest, and
nobody donated? Should we simply revert to the law of the jungle? (I mean
the one about the strong surviving at the expense of the weak, not Kipling's
Law of the Jungle ("which is as Old and as True as the sky, and the wolf
that shall keep it will prosper, but the wolf that shall break it, must
die..."))

This post is not intended to undermine or cast aspersions in any way on the
fine young people (of whatever country's Armed Forces) who are the first to
give their lives in the defence of their way of life.

I just wish they were directed by people with more sense. (I'm not
necessarily talking about the Military Command here, although, they get it
wrong often enough to not be above criticism...the problem is when political
leaders commit young lives for doubtful goals.)

As an ex-soldier myself, I remember what it's like when you're twenty years
old and someone puts a powerful weapon in your hand.  (I couldn't wait to
get to Viet Nam and utilise what I'd been taught...so Gung-Ho it was unreal.
Fortunately, I never had to go. I have nothing but respect for those who
did.)

Now, in my late fifties, having enjoyed a rich and varied life (which other
men died in conflict so I could), I realise this is not the solution.

There IS a time when war is inevitable and it is important that all of us
should be up to that challenge. Not least, we should ensure that those in
our Armed Forces are adequately trained, equipped, and funded.

But we have to be VERY sure there is absolutely no alternative.

Besides, war has changed a lot over the centuries... Consider this...

"Then up spake brave Horatius
 The Captain of the Gate
 'To every man upon this Earth
  Death cometh, soon or late.
  And how can man die better
 Than facing fearful odds,
 For the ashes of his Fathers,
 And the Temples of his Gods.' "

Horatius, Herminius, and Spurius Lartius held back the Tuscan army on the
one bridge over the Tiber that led to Rome (it was just wide enough for
three people) while their compatriots sawed it through. The Tuscans were
very sporting about it and sent men in threes against them.

Delightful image after the first three were quickly despatched by our
heroes...

"And none there was amongst them
 Would face that bold attack,
 While those behind cried "Forward!"
 And those before cried "Back!" "

After they lost a few, they sent one of their heroes (the Lord of Luna) who
wounded Horatius severely in the side and received Horatius' sword through
his head for his trouble... As the bridge was about to go, the other two ran
back, but Horatius, hampered by his wound, had to jump in the Tiber and try
to swim, encumbered by his armour. I won't tell you what happened; you can
read it for yourself in Lord MacCaulay's "Lays of Ancient Rome" (and no,
that wasn't Cleopatra <G>).

See, we don't do stuff like that any more... As we have degraded the
"nobility" of war you could argue that we no longer have the right to wage
it... (however, it is a philosophical argument and probably not worth the
effort of minds that can post several hundred mails to a thread about the
origins of Language (all of which is easily ascertainable in 5 minutes with
a decent encyclopaedia and/or GOOGLE), and their possible connection to
COBOL...<G>)

Pete.
```

```
Yes Daniel's thoughts were expressed with sincerity. And as you pointed out,
when we were younger, possibly we thought along similar lines about the war
machine. But I would love to see a US school text on world history, to establish
whether there is any bias. I'll bet there is.

As an illustration. In my time we had an official set of texts for UK history. -
BUT- I didn't read the identical texts that you would have done at an Anglican
or  non-denominational school. We read the same texts but the specifically 'anti
Papist' bits were removed. That's not to say they were censored. They still
accurately reported history, but excluded the 'digs' inserted by the protestant
authors against 'Romanism'. I can't remember specifics, but you will appreciate
how a protestant author might play up the fact that Guy Fawkes was a
catholic.(He was the only sensible guy to set foot in the House of Commons <G>).

Loved your take on war <G>

You touched on the topic in passing - an alternative to war. This is where
'Western' nations have been sadly morally lacking. As has been demonstrated only
too well, both the League of Nations and United Nations, (both 'incorporated' by
the 'West') have been completely impotent as regards war or staving off wars.

The UN does a good administrative, international job for things like UNESCO, WHO
etc., but is absolutely kiboshed on preventing aggressive acts  Ignore the
rights and wrongs of Gulf War 2, but it was political interest, ( MONEY) which
came into play with the French and Russians trying to stop Resolution 1441 being
used by the Coalition of the Willing. Quite simply, if Iraq Saddam style was
replaced by 'Iraq Model 2', they would have no call on the huge gobs of money
they poured into Saddam's regime. (Even that's complicated; go back to the
'eighties, and the US was dishing out money to Saddam as a counter to the
Iranian revolution).

To get Iraq Reconstruction going, the US and UK have just fine tuned their
latest proposal to the UN to be accommodating to the French and Russians - 'You
will be able to make a claim on what the previous regime owed you from future
generated oil revenues". Seems a real pity to me - much better if the Iraqis
were given a clean slate.

However as my Scottish buddy suggests, as a counter to losing that old regime's
debt, the Russians, (as the current second largest producers), could turn on
the  taps and release real chap oil - truly hurting the Saudis and their Wahhabi
fundamentalists. The U.S. needs that situation like a hole in the head, with its
attendant destabilization of the Saudi monarchy.  Politics, politics,
politics.....

As to the morality of the West -

1931 - Japanese aggression in China - not a damn thing done
1930s - Nazis come to power and well prior to WW2 'labour camps' introduced.
While not 'refined' to the degree of the Holocaust, both US and UK intelligence
were feeding back data to their governments.

Side step WW2, (Chamberlain doing his tippy-toeing through the German
Chancellery)  - pass on to subsequent - Cambodia, the dismemberment of
Yugoslavia resulting in the ethnic/religious cleansings, the murderous loss of
life amongst the blacks in Africa, and our very latest - we have now discovered
the 'Killing Fields' in Iraq.

In the West we saw all this coming before many of the events occurred. Much
debating in the UN, but nothing effective ever came out of the deliberations,
and proposals always snarled by a particular country's fear of a precedent being
set. With an eye on Tibet, our Chinese friends were never enthused about the
concept of toppling totalitarian regimes. Same of course with the previous USSR.

With the Iron Curtain threat no longer in existence, one would have thought the
UN could function in a more meaningful manner. But now we find the world split
into groups like Third World, seething with resentment at First World affluence,
and the latest, Arabic nationalism + Islamic fundamentalism.

I can't offer a solution. But one thing's for sure. The structure and operation
of the UN has to alter. Until the structure changes the only recourse,
reluctantly,  is to go to war. War is never a solution because it occurs after
tragic events have already happened.

To suggest that we could have a world without wars is a Utopian dream. As
Desmond Morris points out, the howling fans supporting the home team at a soccer
match is nothing more than a safety valve for tribal warfare. Now there's a
thought. Europeans, Arabs, Africans, Asians, Aussies, Kiwis, S. Americans all
play soccer.
Now if we could persuade our American cousins to play *real* football (soccer) -
that might be a start. Problem is of course, they'd get into a hell of a fight
as to who implements it under their Constitution, the Feds or the States <G>.

Jimmy
```

```
On 16-May-2003, "James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca> wrote:

> To suggest that we could have a world without wars is a Utopian dream

Yep.   Throughout history the relationship between governments has been like the
relationships between gangs and gang members, or maybe members of a wolf pack.
 The big guy sets the rules and pushes the little guys in line.   The gang
members say they're on the little guy's side, when they can do so without risk.
 If the little guy does fight, he doesn't do it mano a mano, that would be crazy
- so when he fights dirty (by the big guy's definitions), the big guy criticizes
him for being a coward.   Occasionally the top dog wolf gets nipped, and to show
his power and keep control, he attacks the first available victim.

A generation later the players are different, but their behaviors are the same.
```

```
Good analogy. 

I work with mutual fund holdings (the stocks the own). This is public
information filed with the SEC on form 13F. 

We're all familiar with mutual funds geared to the middle class. They have Web
pages and 800 numbers. They invest in recognized brand names such as Microsoft,
Dell, Toys R Us, Lowe's and Amazon. Names which give middle class investors a
warm fuzzy feeling. 

There is another class of mutual funds that's bigger than Vantage 500 and
Fidelity Magellan ($50B). They don't call themselves mutual funds, don't have
Web pages nor 800 numbers, you've never heard of them. They are managed by
'perservation of capital' companies with lineage to Rothschild/Rockefeller/JP
Morgan. In other words Old Money. To participate, one must submit to an
in-person interview, meet the minimum requirement -- typically $1M or at least
$100K plus current income. It is instructive to see which investments give Old
Money a warm fuzzy feeling. It's Lockheed-Martin, Grumman, Exxon, Mobil --
weapons and oil. 

The brief Iraq war was about weapons and oil. We did it for rich people.
```

```
community college.  I was assigned to Student Services, which does 
registration, ID cards, etc.  Whenever I handed someone their blank 
(pre-photo) card, I asked them to sign it and verify their social 
insecurity number.  You'd (or you might not) be surprised how few people 
caught that.  (Of the ones who did, the people who were most amused were 
mid-50s ladies...)

all-volunteer force.  (And besides, unless you're going to pop the

That's what people told me over 10 years ago, and if anything I've

> Sadly, there are always those in our Society who simply cannot "earn their
> keep in a free market".
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> children to their birthright, why not Society protecting its "children"?)

A hand up, not a hand out.  People's positions in life are a direct 
result of the choices they made.  In an affluent society, I have no 
problem helping out once for a short period of time (government charity, 
if you will).  The problem with this is that very quickly it becomes an 
"entitlement".  Why should some 18-year-old burger-flipper buy me 
prescription drugs just because I was foolish with my money and didn't 
invest properly so I would have it when I needed it?  They shouldn't - 
I'm not "entitled" to prescription drugs for free just because I reach a 
certain age.

> Suppose that all Social programs were based on charity as you suggest, and
> nobody donated? Should we simply revert to the law of the jungle?

Why would nobody donate?  People generally feel much more benevolent to 
other people than they do toward their government (relatives excluded).

> There IS a time when war is inevitable and it is important that all of us

Yes - and yet it's difficult to convince some people that this 
preparation must be done during peacetime...  I'm not sure how much of 
the Clintonian slashing of the military you were privy to down under, 
but the US military is much "leaner" than it used to be.

…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> COBOL...<G>)

getting tired of alphabet soup.  Of course, in a political discussion,
```

```
LX-i wrote:

I had also assumed the US military was well paid, (well GI Joe in the UK during
WW2 was considered as such - 'Over-paid, over-sexed and over here' <G> - but
probably the over-paid bit was relative to their income compared to a Tommies
pay). I was surprised to see your reference and Joe's to military pay. Certainly
during my time the pay *was* poor and always lagged behind civilians in catch-up
increases.

Also surprised me that Pete Dashwood started out with a military career. An
absolute non-starter, he would just have been too much of a rebel <G>. While no
rebel, I couldn't hack the stupidity, and as part of the British caste system -
the SNOBBERY. So although twice recommended for a cadetship to RAF Cranwell (our
RAF equivalent to Sandhurst or West Point), and subsequent to that again
recommended for a Permanent Commission -  I'm real glad I didn't get a
commission - I would have been a square peg in a round hole.

I did my stint 1948 to 1961, (becoming an apprentice required me to sign on for
the long haul), then got the hell out of it. Apart from giving up the best
(youthful) years of my life, I would sum up my military career in one word -
B-O-R-I-N-G.   I would not make a good poster boy for military advertising.

…[3 more quoted lines elided]…

Ike's phrase ' the military industrial machine', they'd LUV you for saying the
military should be planned on 10 times a Gulf War. He denies it vociferously,
but Rumsfeld wanted Gulf War2 on the cheap, and claims Tommy Franks got all he
needed. I very much doubt that and it did get tricky there for a while - the
supply lines from Qatar/Basrah heading north were not protected, obviously due
to lack of manpower. (We will only confirm this from the history books some 10
years hence).

While I wouldn't attempt to put a size on US military strength, can't help but
feel it is overblown, given there is no longer a USSR. Perhaps some of the
military budget might be better spent on FOREIGN POLICY, looking inwards first
at some of the  US corporations and their over-aggressive foreign trading
practices, (invisibly) supported by the potential of US military might. (That
last is not a black and white, but an attempt to paraphrase without going into
umpteen paragraphs).

The Western colonialism of the Middle East started at the end of WW1.
From 1918 until roughly 1950 - the whole of the MIddle East was British turf,
also some French, as in Syria. Never been to Iraq but from my days in Egypt
(51/54), I remember names like Basrah, Sharjah (that might have been Jordan) and
one which didn't come up during Gulf War 2, Habbaniyah - my guess that's what we
now call
Baghdad International Airport.

Subsequent to 1950 the same game was being played, just that the Westerners took
on different nationalities over the years. Consider from recent history, Shah of
Persia and replacement by the fundamentalist Iran. Then the hatred of Osama bin
Laden, railing against the US but also the utter contempt for the Saudi royal
family. US business(foreign polic) interests led to both situations. We can see
with hindsight that a different perspective in US foreign policy *might* have
avoided both - most importantly 9/11.

Now you statement, "That way, you not only have the power to send a
message, you have the power to say, "And to the rest of you..."

Careful now ! I do hope your, "And the rest of you ..", is solely directed at
any other non comprehending rag-heads in the Middle East. If unfortunately it it

here spread around the globe will get real pissed off  !.
```

```
Good thinking Batman ! Around about '57 at Northolt I was talking to another

(British Empire Medal), for work he had done maintaining radar equipment when the
Russkies sealed off Berlin - the Berlin Airlift. (JFK, 'Ich bin ein Berliner ').

The RAF also had 'On the JOb Training' for tecchies, (which I'm sure you have), where
they went off to manufacturers for on site training - the only name immediately coming
to mind, Plessey. Obviously the scheme had an attraction to both the military and the
manufacturers, plus the latter were keeping their eyes peeled for the 'bright ones'.
He too had immediate offers of three jobs as he left.

I nearly forgot, at the age of 30 he was a flight sergeant, (three chevrons/stripes
and a crown). That ws quite remarkable; normally not until you were well over 35 +.
The English caste system - he was awarded the BEM. Now had he been an officer it would
have been the MBE (Member of the British Empire), referred to as 'My Bloody Effort' as
opposed to senior officers, say wing commander, (Lt. Col)  upwards, where they would
be awarded the OBE (Order of the British Empire), also referred to as 'Others Bloody
Effort'.
```

```

"James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca> wrote in message
news:3EC7F47C.2A64546E@shaw.ca...
<snip>
>
> Also surprised me that Pete Dashwood started out with a military career.
An
> absolute non-starter, he would just have been too much of a rebel <G>.

I was conscripted, Jimmy. It wasn't a career choice. I was already
programming NCR 315s at the time and had recently married. They had a ballot
system where you registered at age 19 (I was late, and registered at age 20)
and could be called up  the following year.

They pulled the birth dates out of a barrel. I was born at midnight so I
technically had two chances...<G> I complained about this when I registered
and the guy there said: "OK, pick one..." I chose the 20th. They had the
ballot and pulled out the 20th (but the 21st was never extracted...fickle
finger of Fate, I guess <G>). I managed to get it deferred for a year but by
the time I was 22 there was no avoiding it. (Well, I guess I could've gone
to live in Australia, but I considered the Army (or working at
MacDonald's... or "anything") to be preferable to doing THAT <G>).  I was
kind of hoping there might be a change of Government and they would abolish
it (there was, and they did, but it was too late, I was already in camp...).

They gave us a couple of weeks of basic, then IQ and personality tests and
took the top 5% to OCTU for Officer training. The deal was that if we wanted
to, we could join up full time with a commission. In the meantime we were
"acting" officers (I don't know about the others, but I should've got the
Academy Award for my performance <G>...)

As for being a rebel, I guess that is fair. I don't rebel against Authority
as such, but I do like to explore alternative ways of doing things. (They
had the results of my personality tests and they still took me...I guess
they were really desperate <G>)

As I said before, I actually enjoyed (for the most part; some of it was
bloody awful) my time in the military and I look on it as a positive
experience in my life. If we had been required to go to Viet Nam (we were
trained as if we WERE going, but NZ never sent conscripts (Oz did...it was
tragic.) I was definitely up for it... (My wife was having kittens at the
thought and my military service put quite a strain on our marriage; I wasn't
there and she needed to have me there...another story).

Pete.
```

```
In article <3EC7F47C.2A64546E@shaw.ca>,
 "James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca> wrote:

> I had also assumed the US military was well paid, (well GI Joe in
> the UK during WW2 was considered as such - 'Over-paid, over-sexed
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> catch-up increases.

I had a disturbing realization while doing my taxes in mid-1991.  Total 
compensation for my service was slightly more than the current minimum 
wage.  Were actual hours worked used, and considered for overtime 
purposes, it would have been far less.

My monthly pay was about $700 for Desert Shield/Storm.

It took hazardous duty pay to bring the total up to "slightly more", 
without it it would have been "slightly less".  This is the $110 that 
the US government pays its troops when they are faced with the prospect 
of grevious bodily harm.  In my case it was a bit less than 1/6 of my 
monthly pay.

I mentioned this to my father at a later date -- surprise -- he was paid 
the same amount of hazardous duty pay in Korea in 1950.  At the time, 
his monthly pay was about $40, making the combat pay a 300% raise.  In 
40 years the powers that be had not see fit to alter it.

Somewhere in the middle of that Congress got around to raising the 
hazardous duty pay -- to $150/month.

Overpaid?  Not!
```

```
Paul Raulerson wrote:

> <grin> Well, at the risk of showing a U.S. Centric attitude, I don't much care what other

Generally speaking our health care system does work well, although like any social program it does have
its bumps and warts. Here in Alberta within recent months, a 21-year old taken seriously ill, (I think
something like appendicitis - but I'm not sure). He lived in the boonies was shipped to Calgary and
shuffled from hospital emergency ward to emergency ward. (Bit like Christmas, 'There was no room at the
inn.........'). Then taken back to his town hospital in High River and subsequently died. OK, so it was an
administrative cock-up - but lack of facilities, through lack of funding, were contributory.

No different to the States - constant argie bargie between the Feds and provinces. By constitution
provinces are *responsible* for health along with education. Feds as the national tax gatherers dish out
funds. They have progressively reduced their financial involvement, putting the onus on the provinces to
find their own funding  to make it work, (i.e. Provincial Sales Tax - which we don't have in Alberta
because of the gobs of oil).

There is never a finite solution to welfare programs; start out with a philosophical approach and then
figure out can you fund it, and can you keep funding it ! Naturally the 'Left', (NDP) says "Pour more
money in", while the 'Right' like our Fraser Institute Think Tank in Vancouver says, "Privatize it". The
latter argument not being dissimilar to, 'Get rid of all taxes and you can rest assured the wealthy upper
10%  will make the economy boom".

>

think that would be correct for our current royal member contributing to this Newsgroup <G>
```
