```
Your rough calculation is off by way more than that.  The CBO lists 
numbers on the "historical budget data" at http://www.cbo.gov.  All 
numbers are in P(9).

Non-Discretionary spending (aka Entitlements) for 2002 (Table 9)

 � Social Security            452.5
 � Medicare                   253.7
   Medicaid                   147.5
 � Other means-tested         138.5
 � Retirement & Disability     96.1  �  
  �Other non-means-tested      70.8 
   Unemployment Comp           50.6  �  
   Farm Price Supports         13.9  �  
   Deposit insurance           (1.0)

The grand total for all of this spending that is done using a twisted 
interpretation of the interstate commerce clause or the and "other 
things necessary" catch all of article 2, section 8, is 1.2 trillion.

Discretionary spending for 2002 (Table 7):

   Defense        348.9
   International  26.2
   Domestic       359.2 

Much of the discretionary spending is also in the above category of 
being constitutionally questionable.  But this grand total is 734 
billion.

Even if I stipulate that all discretionary spending has proper 
constitutional mandate the level of entitlement spending is 62%.

(These guys make Enrons accounting department look lame.  The numbers 
listed in the entitlement line items are just the checks mailed out -- 
they hide all the 'administration fees', 'personnel', 'buildings', etc 
for the entitlement programs inside domestic discretionary spending.  
Easily the other 8% I claim, probably more.)

The CBO also lists 171 billion of debt service to bonds (Table 5), 
mostly purchased by the Social Security Administration -- required by 
law to "invest" its current surpluses only in government bonds.  

(This is an accounting trick that makes Enron and Worldcomm look 
amateur.  The federal government forces itself to buy bonds with any 
excess funds that it is prohibited by law from spending.  Then it takes 
the bond income, which it is allowed to spend, and spends it.  But it  
gives itself an IOU that agrees to pay itself back, with interest.

My statement of "senior citizens and welfare mothers" fits the top four  
line items of entitlement spending.    Those first four line items 
comprise an outlay of 992.2 billion of 1196.6 billion entitlement 
spending.  Since when is it improper to generalize about something based 
on an 83% sample?

Ok, I've posted official numbers supporting my claims.  Can you come up 
with some official numbers to support your claim that 94% of all taxes 
are used for something else???
```

```
Granted that the figures are a bit dated... but consider what the Boston 
Globe had to say in 1996, from

http://www.corporations.org/welfare/globe2.html

--begin quoted text:

Every year, an estimated $150 billion - in the form of direct federal 
subsidies and tax breaks that specifically benefit businesses - is 
funneled to American companies. Critics call it ``corporate welfare.'' 

The $150 billion for corporate subsidies and tax benefits eclipses the
annual budget deficit of $130 billion. It's more than the $145 billion 
paid out annually for the core programs of the social welfare state: Aid 
to Families with Dependent Children (AFDC), student aid, housing, food and 
nutrition, and all direct public assistance (excluding Social Security and 
medical care). 

[snip]

The subsidies range from $1.4 billion annually in price supports for large 
sugar farming interests; to nearly $2 million to help McDonald's market 
Chicken McNuggets in the Third World; to $20,000 for golf balls that 
defense manufacturer Lockheed Martin billed the federal government as an 
``entertainment'' expense. 

[snip]

After World War II, the nation's tax bill was roughly split between 
corporations and individuals. But after years of changes in the federal 
tax code and international economy, the corporate share of 
taxes has declined to a fourth the amount individuals pay, according to 
the US Office of Management and Budget.

--end quoted text

>
>There are those that describe any payment from government to corporation 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>that will never be used by anyone comes to mind as a perfect example.

Something along those lines, yes.  The government needs pencils, for 
example, and those are usually bought from corporations.  The government 
does not seem to need to defray the costs of mergers and acquisitions, 
which it pays for by tax-breaks.
```

```
The way the program works, if a corporation will set aside an acre, and 
maintain it, as a public park, they will receive a tax break on that 
acre.  The idea is to make industrial parks look nice and be useful to 
the public.  

Several corporations around here have set up fountains, picnic tables, 
well landscaped areas for free public use.  There is even one with a 
nice bicycle/roller-blade/jogging path.

The city and state recives value (landscaping service, land, security, 
reduced parks and rec budget, lower concentrations of emissions, etc), 
but many call this "corporate welfare".

The fact that the government asked them for some service (set aside 
land, locate in a depressed area) should be enough to free them from the 
charge of "corporate welfare", but often that little fact is ignored.  
That is my first test for corporate welfare.

As to mergers and acqusitions, well, why does the government help you 
defray the cost of your house (via interest payment deductions)?  The 
grouping is somewhat unclear...are we talking about interest on 
financing to buy other businesses?  Government often uses tax breaks to 
encourage behaviours it approves of (like home ownership).  I will 
suggest my second test for corporate welfare -- if the break is applied 
equally to all entities, it is not welfare.

How do you feel about these two criteria?  

It is not welfare if:

   It is provided in response to government request.
   It is given equally to all and not a super-targeted break.

Would you add others?
```

```
and admitted it, a cite from the tax code allowing such welfare; etc.

"www.cato.org" will necessarily draw extra scrutiny.  Though in a pinch, 
I would accept a fact if two obviously competing agenda groups (like 
Corporations.org -&- Cato.org) agreed on said fact.  But primary sources 
are better, and anonymous sources like "unnamed critics" are still a 
no-no.
```

```
Now, as for Mr Sennott's sources... I don't know what *you* consider to be 
'diligent research', Mr Zitzelberger, but I have a tendency to perform 
what might be called 'dilettante research'.  I checked the Boston Globe's 
website and sent off an email to their Editorial Department, asking them 
for Mr Sennott's email address... by the time they replied I'd also found 
it myself, posted in a variety of places on the Web.

(I will now violate email etiquette by posting private mail publicly)

I sent Mr Sennott an email, asking:

--begin quoted text:

Good day!  I was researching some data on corporate welfare and I came
across a series you wrote in 1996.  I know this was a few years ago but I
was wondering if you could answer two short questions as to the source of
your date:

1) The CBO (Congressional Budget Office) reports $196B spent on
means-tested social programs (e.g. Welfare) in 1996 while you reported a
figure of $145B.  It is understood that CBO figures are subject to
revision after issue; what was the source of your figure?

2) You reported a figure of $150B as the amount of "corporate welfare";
you compare this to two figures, $167B which you said was "too high" and
$75B, which you did not use.  How did you arrive at the figure of $150B?

I thank you for your time in considering this request; it is greatly
appreciated!

--end quoted text

... and a few hours later I received a reply:

--begin quoted text:

Hi. I am now based in London and for the five years previous was based in
Jerusalem, so all of those notes and that data are far from my memory.
Unfortunately, any notes or back up documents I would have saved from that
series would be in storage. I am sorry to be of more  help.
Charles Sennott
Boston Globe - London Bureau

--end quoted text

... and there you have it, Mr Zitzelberger.  Nothing whatsoever to back up 
the figures except the status of their publication... and besides, too, 
those figures are quoted by... You Know What Kind of organisations, as 
well.

DD
```

```
In article <bag3eh$n6l$1@panix1.panix.com>, docdwarf@panix.com wrote:

> them, say, a presumption of accuracy.

The figures made it by the fact-checkers at the Boston Globe.  The paper 
was rocked by several scandals for fabrications a few years after the 
story in question.

Two star columnists, one a Pulitzer nominee, were both canned for 
repeated, blatant fabrication and plagiarism.  The questions always was 
-- where were the fact-checkers.  In both cases it took external 
entities to raise the red flag.

The only reason I remember this five year old scandal is because it is 
back in the headlines once again.  Joseph Blair was recently busted by 
The New York Times for fabrication of his stories.  He trained as an 
intern for the Boston Globe in the mid-1990s, and the paper is now going 
back and reviewing all the things he did for accuracy.

The Boston Globe had a very severe problem, pervasive, systemic problem 
with fact-checking.  Specifically, that they did not do much of it at 
all.  I like to think that they have gotten their act together and 
cleaned house a bit since the summer of 1998.  But this story ran in 
1996.

In light of the recent coverage of Blair and the historical problems at 
the Globe, I almost thought you were making a joke by posting a story 
from that paper.  That is why I immediately started checking some of 
numbers.

Some, like the sugar subsidy are very easy to check (and it has grown to 
1.9B - 2.0B since), some, as I have stated, are not easy to find.

I would not go as far as put the Boston Globes creditability on par with 
the National Enquirer, but it ranks quite poorly as major media outlets 
go.  You ask for a presumption of accuracy for a story published at a 
paper with obviously broken fact-checking and rampant fabrication by its 
journalists, this is not easy.  Involvement in one scandal would be 
enough to raise serious questions, involvement in three raises a 
presumption of inaccuracy.

Figures of actual dollars spent in 1996 are exactly known by October of 
1996, give or take a few weeks.  By July of 1996 the figure is not know 
exactly, but neither should they be off by 50 Billion, or 25% of the 
total.

How we interpret the number will add value.  But the number itself is 
just the number.


> >I would 
> >think that someone who has condiments that are my age would have learned 
> >to believe no more than half of what he reads.

Nope.  This is a variant of the old saying "Don't believe half of what 
you read, and none of what you hear."  Printing is easy, but it does not 
guarantee truth to the item printed.

> >standards.  Facts must be able to be verified independently via the 
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…

I have checked those and other items.  The only other mention I found 
about the Golf Balls was a broken link, but since it was .00002 billion 
I directed my attention to other items listed.  I am looking for support 
for two figures I find questionable -- in other words, I'm doing my own 
fact-checking.


> >Sources with an obvious bias, such as "www.corporations.org" or 
> >"www.cato.org" will necessarily draw extra scrutiny.  Though in a pinch, 
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> arguments, no... but how good of you to have done so!

If the facts are correct the spin put on them is irrelevant.  If the 
facts are incorrect then it is more properly called propaganda.  When an 
article uses uncited, unsourced figures (e.g. "critics claim it is 
150B") and the article is an obvious favorite groups on the Ralph Nader 
wing of the discussion, I consider it questionable.  

As I said previously, if groups on the opposite end of the discussion 
agree with Naderite groups that 150B is the number then it becomes less 
of an issue.  If they do not agree, and no source for that number can be 
found, I still consider it an issue.  In all cases, an actual source is 
best.

My problem with a 'social justice' award is the very nature of 
journalism.  A journalist is supposed to report on the world.  A 
journalist that is trying to change the world is something different 
entirely.


> >> [snip]
> >>

hard to interpret without facial expressions, voice tone, etc.  I 
originally read it as a "here he goes again..." sort of comment.  It 
certainly looked like one, but I'm experience speaking with royalty  is 
quite limited and I am sure I am wrong, please forgive my presumption.



> ... and there you have it, Mr Zitzelberger.  Nothing whatsoever to back up 
> the figures except the status of their publication... and besides, too, 
> those figures are quoted by... You Know What Kind of organisations, as 
> well.

Every organization that wishes should be able to use those facts and 
figures, regardless of their political leanings.  My concern is with 
organizations of one political leaning using figures that have no  
support.

To promote a non-fact into a fact, simply because it has been printed 
somewhere, is fraught with problems.  Printing is cheap Mr. Dwarf.
```

```
group in preparation for the Iraq invasion was the Office of Special Plans in the

hawks Rumsfeld, Wolfowitz and Perle.

year odd sentence hanging over his head in Jordan for financial finagling), and
his exiled opposition group, the Iraqi National Congress added to the self
delusion.>

<Concludes - original reasoning for GW2 was WMD but is now shifting to the fact
Saddam was a monstrous dictator>"

******

So you know where I'm at . I supported invasion of Iraq, but NOT based on WMD,
rather that we already *knew* Saddam was a monster, and in the long term things
could only get worse - but it was Daddy Bush who should have taken him out 10
years back.

Reading the article above, I discern no "media bias" and for my money it is fact
rather than fiction. Seeing the body language on TV of the three cowboys referred
to above in the White House - I have no problem with the assertions.

Told ya. U.S.A. will be SOL on credibility if you don't turn up those WMDs.

Jimmy
```

```
build an infrastructure to attract the right kind of jobs to our neighborhood.
The difficulty is in defining "our neighborhood".
```

```
years. Instead we should raise the FICA and Medicare tax rates to finance those
six years.
```

```
The ssa.gov website puts a good spin on this -- in fact the same spin 
that you are trying to put on it.  But they do cite actual numbers.

They remind us that half of the male population that reaches adulthood 
will reach 65 -- what remains unsaid, but obvious, is that half will not 
reach 65.  Contrast that with todays rate of 3/4 the male population 
reaching adulthood also reaching 65.

I find it interesting that you use numbers for white males.  One of the 
cruelest effects of this program, as Daniel noted earlier, is the 
transfer effect from elderly black males to elderly white females.  I 
call this effect 'Funding Mrs. Daisy's Retirement'.

The programs proponents often claim it is a great benefit to minority 
workers, but they rarely address that sad fact.

I agree that using life expectancy at adulthood is a better measure, but 
using SSA's own numbers, half the original male participants would die 
before ever receiving a single check.  Today, 80ish percent of male and 
female workers will enter the program.  This is a problem for the 
program as designed.

We ought to ditch this pay-as-you-go Ponzi scheme for self-funded 
retirement accounts.


> Dispite medical advances, people still become feeble and retire at approx. 
> 65.  That they live six years longer is no reason to raise the retirement age by 
> six years. Instead we should raise the FICA and Medicare tax rates to finance 
> those six years. 

I have seen estimates that range from raising the FICA to 36% all the 
way to 72% to correct this problem.  Also the famous mere-2.2% increase 
PER YEAR to correct the program.

Which is why I say that roughly 20% of my time is spent in involuntary 
servitude to senior citizens.  (FICA & Medicare either on a 1099 or with 
my pay reduced to allow for employer contributions)
```

```
> > >> here.  Never mind the documentation, for the nonce...  what do you
see has
> > >> having been the cause for Social Security to stop 'paying benefits
five
> > >> and one half years after the average age of death'?
> > >>
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> > You are using life expectancy at birth, which is heavily influenced by
infant
> > mortality. Infant mortality is not a legitimite issue when discussing
> > retirement
> > plan.
> >
> > In 1920, the life expectancy of a 50 year-old white male in the US was
22.2
> > years. In 2000, it was 28.2 .. six years longer.
> >
…[26 more quoted lines elided]…
> retirement accounts.

Perhaps this a source of confusion. Social Security is not
for retirement, it is, as recall, from a book on the subject,
about the loss of income from unemployment. The three
components are: Old Age, Survivors, and Disability; or
OASDI (Old Age, Survivors, and Disability Insurance.)

Only the Old Age (forced retirement) portion could be
replaced by 'self-funded retirement'; but what of the other
components? I am not, and have not been, a 'fan' of Social
Security, it has questionable Constitutional authority and
has been fiscally mismanged by politcians since its inception;
but in comparing Social Security with private schemes, the
cost of other components can not be ignored.

>
> > Dispite medical advances, people still become feeble and retire at
approx.
> > 65.  That they live six years longer is no reason to raise the
retirement age by
> > six years. Instead we should raise the FICA and Medicare tax rates to
finance
> > those six years.
>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> my pay reduced to allow for employer contributions)

And for the Survivors and Disability portion, in involuntary
servitude to youself.
```

```
Mr Brazee, there was another part of this thread where
I suggestted that I was unable to determine how the 'state'
could apply such force.

Given that the question is related to privately funded insurance,
there is the problem of missing payments; that is, should an
individual remain unemployed for an extended period, there
may not be enough money to keep the insurance in force.
A period of unemployment has little, or no effect, on Social
Security benefits.

Even the 'state' cannot require people to keep such insurance
in force, when the individual has no income.
```

```
There are some particulars for disability that are different
than you state. For disability, an individual must have worked
during five of the last ten years, even with 40 credits.

There is no 'insurance part' of Social Security, all three
components are insurance. Because it is all insurance,
then private insurers could provide the same benefits
as Social Security. Whether they could do all parts 'as
easily as' one part, I don't know.

What of the problem of changing carriers? It seems to
me that, due to the life-long duration of the Old Age
benefit, one could never change carriers. A couple
could have two different carriers. If they cannot change
to a common carrier, how is the spousal benefit to work?

There is so much I don't know that I cannot assess 'as
easily as'.
```

```
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> processing the will (dependong upon state law).

There are certain tax effects that, as I understand them, still
treat such funds as retirement for the survivor; and a number
of conditions are attached. The Survivor benefit is different;
but has its limitations, as well. Which provides the best
coverage depends upon circumstances to broad too go into
here.

> Does the idea that all of the money you invest over your working life
> disappears without a trace upon your death?  Don't all of your other
> accounts transfer to the surviving spouse/next of kin?  The only reason
> the survivor benefit exists is because SSA does not transfer.

I have been bothered by that and by many other things concerning
Social Security. The survivor benefit was never of any use for me.
My assests will transfer to my siblings.

> I'm not sure how you turned this lack of ownership into a benefit, it
> certainly does not sound like it to me.

Each of the three components must be seen as a specific
'catastrophic event' that is covered by term insurance. Term
insurance is less expensive to purchase because it has no
residual value. Consequently, we pay less during our lives
than would be required for paid-up policies.

It might help to think in terms of a single person with no next
of kin. Why pay huge premiums or set aside large sums of
money to accumulate wealth during life when, if none of these
'catastrophic events' occurs, no payment will be received?
What if those for whom you provide precede you?

We tend to provide for what we expect to happen and we
look at life through rose-colored glasses. Social Security,
for all its problems, takes care of those who do not or can
not provide for the unexpected.

I think many fail to recognize that the Old Age benefit is not
the same as an investment and that comparing the two
is like comparing apples to oranges.

> How much of SSA funds actually go to disability?  And how is that
> disability defined?

I don't know how much goes to disability. Never looked
for it.

There is, of course, a document,
< http://www.ssa.gov/pubs/10029.html > and PDF pamphlet.

Selected quotes:
"Disability is a subject you may read about in the newspaper,
but not think of as something that might actually happen to you.
But the chances of becoming disabled are probably greater
than you realize. Studies show that a 20-year-old worker has
a 3-in-10 chance of becoming disabled before reaching
retirement age."

"The definition of disability under Social Security is different
than other programs. Social Security pays only for total disability.
No benefits are payable for partial disability or for short-term
disability.

"Disability under Social Security is based on your inability to work.
We consider you disabled under Social Security rules if you
cannot do work that you did before and we decide that you
cannot adjust to other work because of your medical condition(s).
Your disability must also last or be expected to last for at least one
year or to result in death.

"This is a strict definition of disability. Social Security program
rules assume that working families have access to other resources
to provide support during periods of short-term disabilities,
including workers' compensation, insurance, savings and
investments."
```

```
In my state of Georgia, a jointly held property belongs to the remaining 
spouse upon death of one.  I think this is standard community property 
style, but I  have never been divorced or widowered so I cannot say for 
sure.  I imagine states like the P.R.M. would take half the property in 
tax if one spouse simply looked ill...

> > Does the idea that all of the money you invest over your working life
> > disappears without a trace upon your death?  Don't all of your other
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> My assets will transfer to my siblings.


But your SSA benefits that you have paid for will not.  They are 
property of the state and will remain so.  An individual account could 
be willed to your siblings.  Even if you don't make it to 65/67/70, the 
funds could still belong to them.

> > I'm not sure how you turned this lack of ownership into a benefit, it
> > certainly does not sound like it to me.
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> than would be required for paid-up policies.

I consider the "survivor"  benefit a sham and a scam.  If the program 
were based on individual accounts the spouse would own it anyway.  As it 
is they receive less for surviving, and secondary survivors 
(children/siblings) receive nothing after that.

> It might help to think in terms of a single person with no next
> of kin. Why pay huge premiums or set aside large sums of
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> not provide for the unexpected.


On the other hand -- I feel somewhat slighted.  I know that social 
security will be in great trouble when I reach the age -- thus I fund a 
401k as well as I can.  I know that I may never reach an age of 
retirement, thus I keep life and disability insurance to protect my 
family.  Why exactly is this hard for the average Joe to do also?

I spent 14 years getting (and funding) a college education, with 
pit-stops and breaks for the likes of Kuwait City and Mogadishu.  I have 
worked many 10, 12 and even 16 hour+ days -- (many of my government DOE 
days were wrap-around... thanks Robert W.).  Often I have left a long 
day at the SECOND job to attend school.  On occasion I have even held 
the THIRD, though short lived, job.  In all of this I haven't asked 
anyone to provide for me.  Why should I expect less of anyone  who is 
physically and/or mentally able to do the same?

I am quite happy to provide for those that CAN NOT provide for the 
unexpected for themselves.

I am very pissed off that I am forced to provide for those that WILL NOT 
provide for the unexpected for themselves.

Let them do as I have done, or let them not as they desire.  Why should 
I be forced to bear the burden of their poor choices?


> > How much of SSA funds actually go to disability?  And how is that
> > disability defined?

> for it.

SSI disability, while the remaining 5/6 to 6/7 is old age retirement 
benefit.

> Selected quotes:
> "Disability is a subject you may read about in the newspaper,
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> retirement age."


Of course "disability" is anything that keeps them from working for 12 
months or more.

One of my personal favorites comes from my time in the army.  As a squad 
leader it fell to me to make sure my troops met the attendance 
requirements of their  ADAPC program in the civilian alcohol/drug abuse 
counseling programs.  Usually this was a consequence of them being 
caught DUI, or getting otherwise arrested while toasted -- as such I 
often had to drive them to the AA/NA meetings.  I don't think I ever met 
an alcoholic at those functions that wasn't on "disability".

(* disclaimer -- I'm drinking a beer right now.  This begs an obvious 
question -- If I should choose to drink another twenty and decide to 
skip work tomorrow  do you feel a responsibility to replace my income?)

My other favorite in this program is "ADHD" kids.  60 Minutes did a 
great piece on this a few years ago.  Each child able to get a doctor to 
diagnose as "ADHD" is  worth a monthly check.  What is the reason for 
this?  I was "Hyperactive" as a child.  My folks fixed it with a 
combination of spankings and red dye #2 control -- I had no idea that I 
was worth hundreds/month.  I tend to hold the opinion that this is slack 
parenting.

I mention this because I actually do think that _with_the_exception_of_ 
junkies, alcoholics and the ill-defined ADHD child. that disability is 
really a good thing. Even with those groups, it is still only 1/6 of the 
outlay at most.  My general tuning rule is that the 5/6 of the program 
should be tuned first.
```

```
As I explained later in my earlier post, the benefit 'paid for'
is the insurance for a 'catastrophic event'. It is similar
in this respect to automobile insurance. You pay for the
coverage. You either receive a payment for an 'insured loss'
or you don't. The payment doesn't become the property of
the insurance company, forever; it is used to pay for the
'insured loss' of others. The insurance company takes a
portion of premium payments to cover its overhead and
profit -- the government takes a portion of premium payment
to cover its overhead.

>
> > > I'm not sure how you turned this lack of ownership into a benefit, it
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> (children/siblings) receive nothing after that.

If both parents, in their twenties, died in an accident,
their children might have a few thousand of savings to
last for fifteen years, more or less. Survivor benefits
under Social Security would be much greater and last
until they are able to work.

>
> > It might help to think in terms of a single person with no next
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> family.  Why exactly is this hard for the average Joe to do also?

The 'great trouble' for Social Security is the result of
political foolishness. The concept 'actuarially sound' is
foriegn to politicians. Social Security is considered
actuarially sound if its trust fund will not run out of money
during the next 75 years. Private insurance is actuarially
sound if its reserves will not be exhausted, ever.

The average Joe doesn't earn enough money to make
sound financial decisions and exist in a society that
imposes its 'expectations' upon him. One must either
'Keep up with the Joneses' or reject the society that
lives by that standard. In this respect, there is no
difference between the low-income in common society
and a multi-millionaire in a society of billionaires.
High-paid technical workers more easily adapt to
common society than low-paid laborers.

>
> I spent 14 years getting (and funding) a college education, with
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> physically and/or mentally able to do the same?

You cannot, logically, use 'or' in this context. Your question
may be re-phrased as 'Why should I expect less of those who
are not like me?' And, the answer is 'Everyone is not like you.'

From a different view, If you do expect less of some and
expect more of others; are you not expecting from each
according to ability?

>
> I am quite happy to provide for those that CAN NOT provide for the
> unexpected for themselves.

From each according to ability.

>
> I am very pissed off that I am forced to provide for those that WILL NOT
> provide for the unexpected for themselves.

But you are not forced to provide for others. Congress
forces all wage earners to provide for themselves so
that others will not be a burden on you.

>
> Let them do as I have done, or let them not as they desire.  Why should
> I be forced to bear the burden of their poor choices?

I am not sure we view 'burden' the same way or, perhaps,
having the same cause. There is a relationship between
'premiums' paid to Social Security and the amounts paid
for 'insured losses'; that is, your income determines the
payments for your 'insured loss'. If there were no such
relation, then you might argue a 'burden' is incurred by the
transfer to others in your age group.

The 'burden', I find most often characterized, occurred due
to relatively large payments soon after inception. Because
the payments were not based upon actuarily sound principles,
the interest payments to the trust fund never earned enough
to cover 'insured losses'; thus premiums received were
used, instead. This creates a 'burden' on current payers by
effectively transferring income to those in older groups.

This is the 'pay-as-you-go' about which you complained. The
problem was never Social Security as an insurance program,
it was politicians as idiots. The 'unspeakable' solution is to
reduce current and future payments until the trust fund is
actuarily sound. As an iconoclast, I am able to speak the
words, others discuss raising taxes to maintain payments,
and to raise those taxes to inordinate amounts and thus
show that Social Security must be replaced, not fixed.

>
>
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> benefit.

Social Security disability is not the same as SSI.

>
> > Selected quotes:
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
> skip work tomorrow  do you feel a responsibility to replace my income?)

If drinking another twenty blows out enough brain cells
to cause you to become disabled and if the condition
is expected to continue for at least one year, you would
have already paid for the disability benefit and thus I have
no responsibility to replace your income. If you just wanted
to skip work today for any reason, that is your responsibliity.

>
> My other favorite in this program is "ADHD" kids.  60 Minutes did a
…[33 more quoted lines elided]…
> But anything after 12 months is "permanent".

No, once disability is established, a review occurs after
one, three, or seven years to determine if the status has
changed. When the review is to occur depends upon the
nature of the disability.

>
> I have a lawyer acquaintance that does nothing but file appeals to SSA
> for SSI.  He is quite proud of his record.  For about $3500 he can says
> he can get anyone SSI disability.

Again, Social Security disability is not the same as SSI.
See < http://www.ssa.gov/d&s1.htm >.
```

```

Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message
news:bain1s$e9b$1@peabody.colorado.edu...
>
> On 21-May-2003, "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote:
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> "We" are the people who buy IRAs with the understanding that we get tax
breaks
> from it.   When we fill out the forms and take the tax break, we are
actively
> making a choice to accept those conditions.

But you are assuming that Congress has, within its
enumerated powers, the authority to extend such
contractual terms. I do not.

Generally, this is the difference between Madisonian
(strict) and Hamiltonian (liberal) interpretations of the
Constitution. Hamilton argued that the 'general welfare
clause' conferred on Congress powers not otherwise
enumerated in Article I, Section 8. Madison argued
that the 'general welfare clause' only gave authority
to provide (spend money) for the enumerated powers.

Hamilton, in opposing the inclusion of a Bill of Rights,
argued, in "The Federalist", Number 84, that the
absence of an enumerated power meant that Congress
could not exercise the power. He wrote: "For why declare
things shall not be done which there is no power to do?
Why, for instance, should it be said that the liberty of the
press shall not be restrained, when no power is given
by which restrictions may be imposed?"

Congress was delegated a power to tax incomes
but no power regarding matters of individual spending.

Hamilton, in his opinion on 'supreme law', in "The
Federalist", Number 33, wrote: "But it will not follow
from this doctrine that acts of the larger society which
are 'not pursuant' to its constitutional powers, but which
are invasions of residual authorities of the smaller
societies will become the supreme law of the land.
These will be merely acts of usurpation, and will
deserve to be treated as such. Hence we perceive
that the laws of the Union, like the one we have just
before considered, only declares a truth, which flows
immediately and necessarily from the institution of a
federal government. It will not, I presume, have
escaped observation, that it 'expressly' confines this
supremacy to laws made 'pursuant to the Constitution';
which I mention merely as an instance of caution in the
convention; since that limitation would have to be
understood, though it had not been expressed."

It follows, from the above, that Congress may, at its
discretion, defer the accountability of income for tax
purposes since the deferal, in and of itself, does not
interfere with the power of individuals to spend *their*
money for *their* purposes. It also follows, from the
above, that the deferal of accountability for a particular
purpose does interfere with, and thus usurps, the power
of individuals to spend *their* money for *their* purposes.

Therefore, the additional tax, imposed on individuals
for using money from a retirement account for a purpose
other than retirement as defined by the tax laws, is of
questionable constitutionality.

Notice that I used Hamilton's comments to support
my position and did not rely on the Bill of Rights.
There is an argument derived from the Tenth
Amendment's ultimate reservation of power to the
people that denies all manner of Federal schemes.

>
> So what exactly is your agreement with the government on the Constitution?

"[T]o secure the blessing of liberty to" me under the
terms made clear and reasonable by the Constitution.

>   Did
> you agree that the government interprets the Constitution correctly?

No, I have, for more than 25 years, disagreed.

>   When did
> you come to your agreement with the government concerning the
Constitution.

At my birth. The Constitution and its mandate "to secure
the blessings of liberty" is an inviolable birthright.

>  How
> did you and the government make this agreement?

It was made on my behalf, and accepted as an inviolable
birthright.
```

```
Rick Smith wrote:

> Joe Zitzelberger <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote in message
> news:joe_zitzelberger-3C0195.02590921052003@corp.supernews.com...
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> > > here.<snip>

Joe,

A really good interchange between you and Rick on this topic. I commend you for
getting those Mac-Jobs to see you through, rather than being a leech on society.

Certainly doing your bit in Desert Storm and Mogadishu widened your horizons.
But I also truly get pissed off that the corporate world has created the
Mac-Jobs, primarily in the hospitality and retail industries, where for
servitude you are offered Mac-money for Mac-Jobs.  I'm sure it's the same in the
States as in Canada - to help their corporate financial structures, there are
very, very FEW employees who have the designation 'Permanent Staff'. Last time I
saw anything, dear old Wal-Mart had some 15-20 court cases pending from former
employees in the USA, relating to employment practices..

But so much of life is being in the right place at the right time to grasp the
right opportunity. When I left the RAF after 12 years I had nothing, no
semi-pension etc., nor in my case a 'skill'. I was trained in shorthand/tying -
so had it been my inclination I could have pursued the possibility of a court
writer or spend many gloomy hours in the gallery of the House of Commons, taking
notes for Hansard.
No way, thanks very much !

I was doing a correspondence course for Chartered Institute of Secretaries, (now
defunct, I believe). The corporate secretary function has now been split between
Controller and corporate legal adviser. I had an objective to live in Guildford,
Surrey - a pleasant county town and a truly beautiful county. So I applied for a
job with a national dairy company. Absolutely no sweat. Immediately got an
interview and a job starting in accounting. My very *first* application and job.
(Didn't hurt of course that I was studying for the CIS). That was September '61;
forgot about the CIS nonsense. I was into systems analysis in June '63, having
first gotten to Deputy Accountant.

Now if I rewound the videotape of my life and re-ran it in the 21st Century - I
very much doubt it would pan out as it did back then.

It doesn't matter a tinker's cuss whether or not you are a believer in
Christianity, Bhuddism, etc., Atheism or are an agnostic. - 'decent' folks
should live by a generally accepted and justifiable set of morals.

By all means we don't want to encourage slobs to happily accept handouts. Our
individual successes, whether 'self earned' or given a 'helping hand', should
not deter us as a 'family group' from helping those who are less fortunate or
didn't get the breaks. It also follows, regardless of how you try to organize
society with incentives, legislation etc. that a *very small* percentage will be
unemployable.

We should be adult enough to take even the slobs along with us in shaping
society.

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

```
That is the rub.    I believe that the best approach to limiting the
government's power isn't to limit taxes, but is to limit spending.

e.g.  If the state spends more than 18% of the GDP, no department can increase
its budget without 2/3 vote (similar to war).   Simple, nothing gets cut,
emergencies can be handled - but they have to wait for the economy to grow for
the state to grow.
```

```
Here's a link to the IRS web site...

http://www.irs.gov/app/understandingTaxes/js/theme3/3_3_1.html

For those reading offline, here are the rates...
$0       - $27,050  - 10%    ($0      - $2,705)
$27,051  - $65,550  - 27.5%  ($7,439  - $18,026)
$65,551  - $136,750 - 30.5%  ($19,993 - $41,709)
$136,751 - $297,360 - 35.5%  ($48,546 - $105,563)
$297,351 and up     - 39.1%  ($116,264)

Now, 10% is reasonable, in my opinion.  However, notice the jump at $27k 
- over 1/4 of your income goes to the government!  At $136k, it's over 1/3!

Now, let's look at the values associated with these numbers (which I 
added above).  Say you've got a small business, and it's been reasonably 
successful.  You made $250k last year!  Look at how much of that you get 
to keep - barely $150k of it.  Now, to keep this business going, you 
need to invest $100k into this year.  That leaves you with $50k, which 
probably isn't what you had in mind when you thought you'd be a 
successful entrepreneur.

Imagine it was 10% all the way up - instead of making $150k, you'd make 
$225k - much more of an incentive to grow your business then.

>>-That's- the best way to 
>>stimulate the economy, not having the government giving back money they 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> society?

>>You do know that there's no such thing as "corporate taxes", right?  :)
> 
> 
> Just as I know that all money currently in circulation belongs to the 
> Government, sure!

Nope.  Corporations (just like the government) have no money outside of 
what they collect from their consumers (or citizens).  Corporate taxes 
are actually consumer taxes.  There's no free lunch, and there's no 
money tree.
```

- **From:** "JerryMouse" <nospam@bisusa.com>
- **Date:** 2003-05-17T08:26:34-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<VmydnSEhDqgUpVujXTWJjw@giganews.com>`
- **References:** `<4T2dna3ZZYcwY12jXTWJkQ@giganews.com> <3EC42356.9040708@Knology.net> <ba1e63$b0f$1@panix1.panix.com> <3EC4CD9B.3050709@Knology.net> <ba2lku$rj3$1@panix1.panix.com> <3EC598E2.9000105@Knology.net> <JMPlV5b5VFnN-pn2-PVzMOp5FmDdj@h24-82-204-17.wp.shawcable.net>`

```

"Lorne Sunley" <lsunley@mb.sympatico.ca>
>
> If you leave the money in the business rather than paying it to
> yourself as income you will not have to pay taxes on it....

Huh? Where'd you get that silly notion? Sole owners, partnerships, and
Subchapter S corporations are taxed as individuals. If you make a profit,
you pay tax on the profit. "Leaving money in the business" is identically
the same as "leaving money in your pocket."

You CAN deduct from profits any expenses necessary to run the business. You
can 'expense' certain purchases that would otherwise be depreciated, but not
$150,000 worth (the current maximum is $27,500).

So, if your $250,000 revenue yields $150,000 profit, you can "leave" a
maximum of $27,500 in the business (by buying new equipment) and your
taxable income now becomes $122,500.

The tax situation is even worse for Subchapter "C" corporations (the regular
kind).


> > >>-That's- the best way to
> > >>stimulate the economy, not having the government giving back money
they
> > >>shouldn't have taken in the first place.

The government doesn't give the money back to you. The government gives your
money to someone else.
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<QQSdnSqS6-fhGlujXTWJhQ@giganews.com>`
- **References:** `<4T2dna3ZZYcwY12jXTWJkQ@giganews.com> <3EC42356.9040708@Knology.net> <ba1e63$b0f$1@panix1.panix.com> <3EC4CD9B.3050709@Knology.net> <ba2lku$rj3$1@panix1.panix.com> <3EC598E2.9000105@Knology.net> <JMPlV5b5VFnN-pn2-PVzMOp5FmDdj@h24-82-204-17.wp.shawcable.net> <VmydnSEhDqgUpVujXTWJjw@giganews.com> <JMPlV5b5VFnN-pn2-0jFk5dMZsJ6K@h24-82-204-17.wp.shawcable.net>`

```

"Lorne Sunley" <lsunley@mb.sympatico.ca>
>
> From what you are saying, I gather that small business owners here in
> Canada (private corporations) have a much better tax deal than the
> equivalent in the USA...

Sorry for the confusion. In the US, there are (principally) two types* of
corporations: Subchapter "S" and "C" (referring to the appropriate parts of
the tax code).

"C" is your standard corporate model:  Micros~1, General Electric,
Time-Warner, etc.

"S" allows a business to operate as a corporation (limited liability, small
number of shareholders) but pay taxes at the individual rate. The individual
rate is always cheaper than the corporate rate - mainly because you're not
taxed twice: once as a corporation and once as a dividend recipient. The
actual reason is that people vote and corporations don't.

Probably the two - our "S" and your "private" - corporations are much the
same.




----
*In addition, there are several non-profit corporation possibilities.
```

- **Date:** 2003-05-19T14:29:03+00:00

- **Message-ID:** `<j7qhcv83u8k1g4t44o6tah7o5ujpc053nk@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<4T2dna3ZZYcwY12jXTWJkQ@giganews.com> <3EC42356.9040708@Knology.net> <ba1e63$b0f$1@panix1.panix.com> <3EC4CD9B.3050709@Knology.net> <ba2lku$rj3$1@panix1.panix.com> <3EC598E2.9000105@Knology.net> <JMPlV5b5VFnN-pn2-PVzMOp5FmDdj@h24-82-204-17.wp.shawcable.net>`

```
"Lorne Sunley" <lsunley@mb.sympatico.ca> wrote:

[snip]
>
>If you leave the money in the business rather than paying it to 
>yourself as income you will not have to pay taxes on it....
>

Lorne:

I can't speak for the Canadian corporate tax system, but in the US,
you can leave money in the corporation and still be responsible for
personal income tax.  It depends on your corporate subchapter status.

If the corporation is a subchapter S corporation, shareholders are not
personally responsible for taxes on retained earnings.  The retained
earnings are still taxed at the corporate tax rate.

If the corporation is a subchapter C corporation, the shareholders are
responsible for paying personal income tax on the corporation's net
income, regardless of whether the net income is distributed to
shareholders or retained by the corporation.

The advantage of being a "C corp" in the US is that the net income is
not taxed twice as are dividends paid by an "S corp."



Bob Wolfe
~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
When replying by e-mail, make sure that you correct the e-mail address.
Check out The Flexus COBOL Page at http://www.flexus.com
```

```
in the US.

$297,351 and up     - 39.1%  ($116,264)

$29,735,100 @ 10 or 15%. Now so happens there's one family up here,   the

but I doubt its true efficacy as a means of making all contribute fairly. A
definition of 'fairness' could be - are you the pilot, a passenger in business
class, followed by third class, or are you some poor sod hanging on to the
```

```
James J. Gavan wrote:
> $29,735,100 @ 10 or 15%. Now so happens there's one family up here,   the
> Bronfmans who used to own Seagrams booze. (I think they since sold off). Assume
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> the jobs they produced for other people to earn their bread".

Heh - I'm not going to defend shady accounting practices.  That's why I 
advocate a "flat tax", if you will.  (Boy, I'm going to get pegged as a 
Libertarian yet...)  See below...

> The concept of a flat tax is non-debatable, certainly much easier to administer -
> but I doubt its true efficacy as a means of making all contribute fairly. A
> definition of 'fairness' could be - are you the pilot, a passenger in business
> class, followed by third class, or are you some poor sod hanging on to the
> plane's tail !.

To me, the concept of a flat tax is the ultimate in fairness.  If I make 
$20K a year (and I don't make much more - DoD pay scales are public 
knowledge), and I have 2 children (which I do), I not only have no 
income tax liability, I actually get what is called a "refundable" tax 
credit (the Earned Income Tax Credit) to the tune of around $2K a year. 
  But why am I entitled to money from Bill Gates or Larry Ellison or 
Warren Buffett?  If I make $20K a year, I should pay $2K in taxes 
(assuming a flat rate of 10%).

Now, let's look at Bill Gates.  I have no clue what his net worth or 
annual earning are.  But, I'm sure he's in the 39% bracket.  Let's cut 
his rate to 10%.  We'll have to add many lower-bracket taxpayers to make 
up the difference, but that's the way the current breakdown goes.

(My wife thinks I'm crazy that I believe I should have to write a check 
at the end of the year.  But, it's what I believe.  "Fair" is -everyone- 
pitching in their "fair" share, not exempting people because they didn't 
make a whole lot.)  In fact, the whole concept of a progressive 
(graduated) tax scale punishes success.  Can you really tell me that 
it's morally right that Bill Gates isn't entitled to 40% of the money he 
earns?  (Now I know that Bill Gates is probably one of the richest folks 
in the world, but think about your local "business leader".)

I believe that it's not "our" right to determine how much of "their" 
money "they" "need".  :)
```

```
doesn't think of his trophy as belonging to the race organizer, he

> all.

So in response to the above, you'd say, "Well, actually, the trophy 
belongs to the government, since they produced the funds with which the 
race organizer acquired the trophy"?

There are other ways where being the "source" doesn't guarantee you both 
ownership and control.  Think of children - you own yours, but when they 
turn 18, suddenly they become their own person, independent of you.  (Of 
course, you lose control of them the moment they're born...  :> )
```

```
trying to figure out "equitable" taxes), we'll never arrive at the same
```

```
In article <3EC9AE1B.1050506@Knology.net>,
LX-i  <DanielJSNOSPAM@Knology.net> wrote:
>docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
>>>There has -got- to be a better
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>Taxation

All right... what are you seeing as the criteria for 'better' when it 
comes to systems of taxation?

>
>>>So what you're saying is that you see taxes of government taking what's
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>destination.

Given the facts presented - that government determines the supply of
money, that government physically creates and distributes it, that
disposition of it and that government disposes of it when government feels 
the need to... what aspects of 'ownership' are left to anyone else?  How 
can one come to a conclusion that there are aspects of ownership outside 
of these?

(Notice, by the bye, that I am not arguing 'beliefs' or 'my view' or 'This 
I Hold True'... what are presented here seem to be matters of fact)

>
>>>>An increase in circulating money supply without an associated  increase in
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>why would a government intentionally start inflation?

There have been reasons in the past - the classic was 'spending your way
out of debt' - but no matter what the reason the end results with which I
am familiar have been the same, a ruined economy, a devestated currency
and years of reconstruction.

(A delightful side-point in this is the concept of seigniorage, the 
difference between the value of money and the cost to produce it.  Having 
a debt in a currency which one controls is *such* a tricky matter!)

>
>> Each Federal Reserve Bank (there are twelve of them, last I looked) has 
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>really don't mind being proven wrong, as long as I can learn in the process.

The learning is, to me, the most enjoyable part... others here may speak 
of 'winning'; I prefer Jefferson's 'lighted taper' analogy.

>
>> http://www.ustreas.gov/education/fact-sheets/currency/distribution.html
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>ask for it.  That's interesting - a lot can happen in a year.

A lot can be kept from happening, as well... I recall, a few years back, 
some folks were getting a bit riled up because the Fed, anticipating folks 
withdrawing bunches of cash because they feared a collapse of the banking 
system due to Y2K bugs (remember those heady days?), printed up a bunch of 
new currency and put it into warehouses.

Some folks pointed to this as a Sure Sign of inflation and ruin to come; 
no amount of persuasion seemed to be sufficient to convince them that if 
the money never saw circulation it would have no economic effects.

DD
```

```
Joe Zitzelberger <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote:

>In article <FAEwa.297$vk3.68131@twister.austin.rr.com>,
> "Paul Raulerson" <praulerson@hot.NOSPAM.rr.com> wrote:
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
>citizens and welfare mothers.

As a practical matter, elected politicians (not government) are following the
incentives we provided, are 'working from within the system' as we set it up. 

Politicians' first, perhaps only, objective is reelection. The best way to
accomplish that is by building a constituancy who will vote for the candidate or
party because they feel like they got more money than they gave. 

Suppose a party, lets call them Democrats, takes from 20% of the population and
gives to 50% (while ignoring the other 30%). The 20% might be pissed off and vow
to never vote for a Democrat, but the 50% will reelect them. 

In fact, the upper 1% (in income) of the US population pay 36% of Federal income
tax, and 23% of all Federal taxes. The upper 10% pay well over 50% of Federal
taxes. 

http://www.cbpp.org/4-16-02tax.htm

Our system of government encourages higher taxes, higher entitlements, and two
economic classes, with at least 51% of the population in the lower class. This
has nothing to do with idealism, everything to do with pragmatism.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: [OT] Legislative Shenanigans

_(reply depth: 11)_

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com
- **Date:** 2003-05-18T11:44:49-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ba89ph$nso$1@panix1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<4T2dna3ZZYcwY12jXTWJkQ@giganews.com> <3EC5991D.6050903@Knology.net> <ba573p$ghv$1@panix1.panix.com> <3EC7122A.2090403@Knology.net>`

```
In article <3EC7122A.2090403@Knology.net>,
LX-i  <DanielJSNOSPAM@Knology.net> wrote:
>docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
>>>>.... and given the role of corporations in politics... it might be
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>Man - two apologies in 2 messages...  Sorry...

Most gracious of you.

>
>Are you implying by the above that you think it is not good for the 
>economy to encourage business growth?

Implication is in the mind of the beholder, of course... but thanks much 
for causing me to take a closer look at what I am doing, such examination 
can be valuable.

I would say that 'business' is like cutting open a person's flesh... it is 
a neutral act.  A cut in the skin can be done during an assault; this is 
generally considered 'not a good thing'.  A cut in the skin can also be 
done by a surgeon performing an emergency appendectomy in order to save a 
life; this is generally considered 'a good thing'.  The cutting, however, 
is neutral.

Likewise with 'business'... buying, selling, indulging in the exchange of 
goods and services, etc. can be 'not a good thing' or 'a good thing'.

>Or are you just curious as to 
>which political party is generally thought of as more pro-business?

I am aware that, frequently, in the United States of America the 
Republican party is thought of as being more pro-business... but as stated 
above, 'doing business' is a neutral act.  Generating fruits and 
vegetables, for instance, is a 'business', and allowing folks to get food 
more readily and for less cost is generally thought of as 'a good thing' 
while killing songbirds with pesticides is generally thought of as 'a bad 
thing'.

Gets kind of... complicated, no?

DD
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: [OT] Legislative Shenanigans

- **From:** LX-i <DanielJSNOSPAM@Knology.net>
- **Date:** 2003-05-14T18:15:08-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3EC2CDFC.2090806@Knology.net>`
- **References:** `<4T2dna3ZZYcwY12jXTWJkQ@giganews.com> <3EC189B6.3010209@Knology.net> <b9te87$2ih$1@panix1.panix.com>`

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
> In article <3EC189B6.3010209@Knology.net>,
> LX-i  <DanielJSNOSPAM@Knology.net> wrote:
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> system to change it or find another place more to your liking.'

I'm happy with where I am.  (And, like I said, I realize that I'm a 
government employee, although we kept working during the government 
shutdown.)  I'm working within the system to change it, as a Republican 
(not Libertarian).  But, regardless of its odor, you can't argue that 
it's untrue...  :)

My gripe is not with taxes, but with the system.  Governments must 
collect money from its citizens (especially one "of the people, by the 
people, for the people") to fund common services.  However, this list of 
"common services" for which Americans are now taxed has grown into a 
humongous kluge of services, defense, and social programs.  In the 
process, the Federal government controls more and money.

Then, you add on the extreme legislators (on both sides) who put 
behavior caveats on Federal money (i.e., you will not get Federal 
highway funds unless your legal drinking age is 21), and you get a 
Federal government much larger than I believe any of the founders 
envisioned.  (And yes, I know there's nothing "extreme" about my example 
above, but there are others that are.)

I think a smaller government is the way to go (and not Clinton's 
definition of "fewer employees") - a government that does what it must 
to provide for the safety and common defense of its people, and stays 
out of the way otherwise.  Smaller government means a government smaller 
in scope, not personnel (though certainly personnel will follow).
```

```

LX-i <DanielJSNOSPAM@Knology.net> wrote in message
news:3EC2CDFC.2090806@Knology.net...
[snip]
>
> Then, you add on the extreme legislators (on both sides) who put
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> above, but there are others that are.)

It is an example of usurpation and is a violation of the
guarantee clause (Article IV, Section 4) in which "The
United States shall guarantee to every State in this
Union a Republican form of government, ...."

In this case, the people delegate to their States the
power to establish a minimum drinking age. Then for
the United States, to establish a minimum drinking
age by act of Congress, is to renege on the guarantee
clause by denying the power of the people to delegate
who is to establish the minimum drinking age. This
means the act of Congress is unconstitutional to
the extent that it attempts to establish a minimum
drinking age.

The guarantee clause has not been fully tested by the
Supreme Court. Many State cases relied on the Tenth
Amendment, which has not been properly interpreted.

Of course, all of this is my opinion.
```

- **From:** "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>

```

JerryMouse <nospam@bisusa.com> wrote in message
news:EYKcnV0lGL1jl16jXTWJlg@giganews.com...
>
> "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> United States by this Constitution, nor prohibited by it to the States,
are
> reserved to the States respectively, or to the people") is as dead as the
> amendment outlawing alcoholic beverages.
>
> You're correct: The Tenth has not been properly (or ever) interpreted
> because, to the judiciary, it doesn't make a lick of sense.

< http://www.access.gpo.gov/congress/senate/constitution/toc.html >
Document 'con021.pdf', 12 pages.

In the entire discussion of the interpretation of the Tenth
Amendment, there is no reference to powers reserved
'to the people'. The discussion is extensive with respect
to the relations between the United States and the States;
but has nothing concerning the people.

In a Republican form of government, supreme power is
retained by the people. This concept was well understood
and was expressed in the Declaration of Independence.
The words appear in the Constitution, itself. Yet, the Court
seems unable to take the position that
-- The people delegated powers to the United States
-- The people prohibited exercise of some powers by
    the States
-- The people delegated powers to the States
-- The people retained all other powers for their use
    to delegate or exercise at their discretion.

This view of the Tenth Amendment would greatly curtail
the ability of Congress to interfere with the power of the
people to make choices for themselves.

I see the problem as interest groups influencing the
members of Congress to make laws that impose
the views of these groups upon the whole of the
population. Remove the ability of Congress to make
such laws and the bulk of the problem goes away.
```

```
news:kTOdnanMHOa4o1mjXTWcoQ@giganews.com...

> "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…

'special

> Special interests are OK at the state and local level?

Congress was given an extraordinary power to tax,
then arrogated an extraordinary power to spend and
control people.

When special interest groups influence members of
Congress they have a small audience and access to
a lot of money to spend and power to control people.

States don't have nearly as much power to tax and
spend or to control. These powers are better controlled
by the people. There are also many more legislators
to influence.

Thus, the ability of special interest groups to force
the people to adhere to their views is reduced.

Restoring the limits on Congressional power will
force special interest groups to move to the state
and local level where they can be watched more
closely.
```

```
The National government of the United States extends
externally to the relations with other nations.

The Federal government extends internally to the States.

Hence, the phrase should be 'unconstitutional Federal
speed limit'.

to blur the distinction between the National and Federal
governments. By 1937, the distinction all but disappeared.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: [OT] Legislative Shenanigans

_(reply depth: 7)_

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2003-05-15T15:50:27-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ba15jj$sdt$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<4T2dna3ZZYcwY12jXTWJkQ@giganews.com> <3EC189B6.3010209@Knology.net> <b9te87$2ih$1@panix1.panix.com> <3EC2CDFC.2090806@Knology.net> <vc5qtaf2rfc85e@corp.supernews.com> <ba0868$7ho$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <vc82arptui0d04@corp.supernews.com>`

```
Problem I see with this whole discussion is that what is constitutional at
any given moment is precisely what the Supreme Court decides is
constitutional at that moment.  It's not clear to me that the concept of
"judicial review" is itself constitutional, however clear it might have been
to Marshall in 1803.  The only ways we, as citizens, have to decide in any
sort of semi-permanent way what is constitutional and what is not is or not
are through amendments to the constitution.  Though reversal of a final
constitutionality decision on a given topic might be rare, it's not
unthinkable; generally speaking, if the Supreme Court decides that a law
(federal or otherwise) making cutting your toenails on Thursday a capital
crime is constitutional, it's constitutional, until such time as somebody
can convince the justices to revisit the issue *and* reverse the prior
ruling.   Regardless of whether *you* or *I* think such a law is
constitutional or not.

    -Chuck Stevens


"Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote in message
news:vc82arptui0d04@corp.supernews.com...

>
```

```
"Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote in message
news:ba15jj$sdt$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com...
> Problem I see with this whole discussion is that what is constitutional at
> any given moment is precisely what the Supreme Court decides is
> constitutional at that moment.  It's not clear to me that the concept of
> "judicial review" is itself constitutional, however clear it might have
been
> to Marshall in 1803.  The only ways we, as citizens, have to decide in any
> sort of semi-permanent way what is constitutional and what is not is or
not
> are through amendments to the constitution.  Though reversal of a final
> constitutionality decision on a given topic might be rare, it's not
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> constitutional or not.

Exactly.

There's no such thing as an 'unconstitutional law,' for if it's
unconstitutional, it's not a law.

Laws don't get born unconstitutional - they get denominated as such by the
courts.
```
